Default Ruling of Indefinite Nouns in Insha' and Akhbar
Stating the default ruling for indefinite nouns outside special contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the default ruling of an indefinite noun (nakira) in insha' and akhbar contexts, and apply the Hanafi repetition rule to determine whether a repeated indefinite refers to the same or a different entity.

Core Idea
In akhbar, a nakira indicates a single unknown; in insha', it is absolute. Repetition changes reference based on definiteness.

This lesson introduces the foundational Hanafi usul rule for indefinite nouns outside special contexts like commands and negations. Understanding when a nakira is specific (khass) or absolute (mutlaq) is crucial for deriving correct rulings from Quran and Sunnah. The repetition rule—whether the second mention is definite or indefinite—determines if the same entity is intended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Determine if the nakira occurs in an insha' (command, prohibition) or akhbar (declarative) sentence. This distinction changes whether the nakira is absolute or specific to a single unknown.
In insha', the nakira is mutlaq because it does not refer to a specific known instance.
2
Apply the default ruling
For insha', the nakira is mutlaq; for akhbar, it establishes one unknown individual (fard majhul).
Example: 'I saw a man' identifies an unknown man; 'sacrifice a cow' leaves the cow undefined.
3
Analyze repetition
When a nakira is repeated as indefinite, it refers to a different entity; when repeated as definite, it refers to the same entity, because the definite article indicates prior mention (al-'ahd).
This rule applies regardless of whether the first noun is definite or indefinite.
4
Verify with Quranic examples
Examine the examples: 'As We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, and Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' (indefinite then definite – same messenger) and 'Indeed, with hardship is ease, indeed with hardship is ease' (indefinite repeated – two distinct eases per the rule, but the text clarifies it is emphasis).
Ibn Abbas interpreted the latter as two distinct eases; the preferred Hanafi view is emphasis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وَالنَّكِرَةُ فِي غَيْرِ هَذِهِ الْمَوَاضِعِ خَاصٌ لَكِنَّهَا تَكُونُ مُطْلَقَةٌ إِذَا كَانَتْ فِي الْإِنْشَاء ) نَحْوُ قَوْله تَعَالَى ) إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُكُمْ أَنْ تَذْبَحُوا بَقَرَةً ) . ( وَيَثْبُتُ بِهَا وَاحِدٌ مَجْهولٌ عِندَ السَّامِعِ إِذَا كَانَتْ فِي الْأَخْبَارِ نَحْوُ رَأَيْت رَجُلًا فَإِذَا أُعِيدَتْ نَكِرَةً كَانَتْ غَيْرَ الْأُولَى ، وَإِذَا أُعِيدَتْ مَعْرِفَةٌ كَانَتْ عَيْنَهَا لِأَنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي اللَّامِ الْعَهْدُ ، وَالْمَعْرِفَهُ إِذَا أُعِيدَتْ فَكَذَلِكَ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ) أَى إِذَا أُعِيدَتُ الْمَعْرِفَةُ نَكِرَةً كَانَ الثَّانِي غَيْرَ الْأَوَّلِ ، وَإِنْ أُعِيدَتْ مَعْرِفَةٌ كَانَ الثَّانِي عَيْنَ الْأَوَّلِ فَالْمُعْتَبَرَ نَكِيرُ الثَّانِي ، وَتَعْرِيفُهُ . ( وَقَالَ ابْنُ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى ) فَإِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا) لَنْ يَغْلِبَ عُسُرٌ يُسْرَيْنَ ، وَالْأَصَحُ أَنَّ هَذَا تَأْكِيدٌ ، وَإِنْ أَقَرَّ بِأَلْفِ مُقَيَّدٍ بِصَكَ مَرَّتَيْنِ يَجِبُ أَلْفٌ وَإِنْ أَقَرَّ بِهِ مُنَكَرًا يَجِبُ أَلْفَانِ عِنْدَهُ) أَيْ عِندَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ . ( إِلَّا أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ الْمَجْلِسُ ) فَالْأَقْسَامُ الْعَقْلِيَّةُ أَرْبَعَةٌ فَفِي قَوْله تَعَالَى : كَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ رَسُولًا فَعَصَى فِرْعَوْنُ الرَّسُولَ ) أُعِيدَتْ النَّكِرَةُ مَعْرِفَةٌ ، وَفِي قَوْله تَعَالَى ) إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا) أُعِيدَتُ النَّكِرَةُ نَكِرَةً ، وَالْمَعْرِفَةُ مَعْرِفَةٌ ، وَنَظِيرُ الْمَعْرِفَةِ الَّتِى تُعَادُ نَكِرَةً غَيْرُ مَذْكُورٍ وَهُوَ مَا إِذَا أَقَرٌ بِأَلْفِ مُقَيَّدٍ بِصَكَ ثُمَّ أَقَرَّ فِي مَجْلِسِ آخَرَ بِأَلْفِ مُنَكَرٍ لَا رِوَايَةَ لِهَذَا ، وَلَكِنْ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَجِبَ أَلْفَانِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى .

ترجمہ: اور نکرہ ان جگہوں کے علاوہ میں خاص ہوتا ہے بوجہ ہونے اس کے دلالت کرنے والا فرد پر لیکن وہ ہوتا ہے مطلق جب کہ ہو وہ انشاء میں ) جیسے ان تذبحوا بقرة ( اور ثابت ہوتا ہے اس کے ساتھ واحد مجہول سامع کے نزدیک جبکہ ہو وہ اخبار میں جیسے رائیت رجلا ۔ پس جب اعادہ کیا جائے نکرہ کا تو ہوگا وہ اول کا غیر ۔ اور جب اعادہ کیا جائے اس نکرہ کا معرفہ تو ہوگا وہ اس نکرہ کا عین کیونکہ اصل لام میں عہد اور معرفہ کہ جب اعادہ کیا جائے اس کا تو اسی طرح ہوتا ہے دونوں صورتوں میں )
And the indefinite noun in other than these contexts is specific, but it becomes absolute when it is in insha', like His statement 'Allah commands you to slaughter a cow.' And it establishes a single unknown individual in the listener's mind when it is in akhbar, like 'I saw a man.' Then if it is repeated as an indefinite, it is other than the first. And if it is repeated as a definite, it is the same as the first, because the original use of the definite article (al-) is for prior mention (al-'ahd). And when the definite noun is repeated, similarly in both ways: if the definite is repeated as indefinite, the second is other than the first; if repeated as definite, the second is the same as the first. So the consideration is the indefiniteness or definiteness of the second. Ibn Abbas (RA) said regarding His statement 'For indeed, with hardship [will be] ease. Indeed, with hardship [will be] ease' that one hardship will not overcome two eases. And the more correct view is that this is [mere] emphasis. And if one acknowledges [a debt of] one thousand [dirhams] qualified by a document twice, one thousand is obligatory. And if one acknowledges it as indefinite, two thousand is obligatory according to him – that is, according to Abu Hanifa (RA), unless the gathering is the same. The rational categories are four: In His statement 'As We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, and Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger,' the indefinite is repeated as definite. In His statement 'For indeed, with hardship [will be] ease,' the indefinite is repeated as indefinite, and the definite as definite. And the parallel of a definite repeated as indefinite is not mentioned, and it is what if one acknowledges [a debt of] one thousand qualified by a document, then acknowledges in another gathering one thousand indefinitely – there is no transmitted report for this, but it should be that two thousand is obligatory according to Abu Hanifa (RA).
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage defines the default ruling for indefinite nouns (nakira) when they are not in the specific contexts earlier discussed (such as in siyaq al-amr, negation, etc.). The author states that in other contexts, the indefinite noun is specific (khass), meaning it indicates a single individual. However, when it occurs in a command (insha'), like 'slaughter a cow,' it is absolute (mutlaq) because the command is for any cow fulfilling the conditions. In a declarative sentence (akhbar), like 'I saw a man,' it establishes a single unknown individual. An important rule about repetition is then given: if the same indefinite is repeated as indefinite, it refers to a different individual; if repeated as definite (with al-), it refers to the same individual. This is because the definite article al- implies prior mention (al-'ahd). The same applies when a definite noun is repeated. The passage then cites Ibn Abbas's famous interpretation of two eases with one hardship, noting that the more correct view is that the repetition is for emphasis, not to indicate two distinct hardships. The discussion concludes with a fiqh application: in cases of iqrar (acknowledgment), if someone acknowledges one thousand qualified by a document twice in separate gatherings, only one thousand is obligated (because the qualification indicates the same debt). If acknowledged indefinitely twice, two thousand are obligated according to Abu Hanifa, unless the gathering is the same (in which case it is one acknowledgment). This demonstrates how the rules of definiteness and indefiniteness affect legal consequences.

Key Terms
نكرة (nakira): An indefinite noun that indicates a single unknown individual in akhbar, or is absolute in insha'.
انشاء (insha): A speech act creating a new state, like commands and prohibitions.
اخبار (akhbar): A declarative sentence reporting a fact.
عهد ('ahd): Prior mention or known reference; the basis for the definite article indicating the same entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule, if someone says 'I saw a man' and then later says 'I saw the man,' what does the second refer to?
A) The same man (because definite).
B) A different man.
C) Ambiguous.
D) Cannot determine.

Key Takeaway
An indefinite noun in akhbar indicates a single unknown; in insha', it is absolute. When repeated, definiteness determines reference.

Fiqh Application of Nakira Repetition: The Case of Acknowledgment (Iqrar)
Applying the indefinite noun repetition rule to iqrar cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of indefinite noun repetition to the fiqh case of iqrar (acknowledgment) and determine the number of obligations based on definiteness and context.

Core Idea
In iqrar, repeating an indefinite acknowledgment in separate gatherings creates multiple obligations; repeating a qualified acknowledgment does not.

This lesson connects the usul rule about indefinite noun repetition to a practical fiqh scenario: acknowledging a debt. Understanding whether the acknowledgment is definite or indefinite, and whether it is in the same gathering or different, determines how many units are owed. This demonstrates how usul principles directly shape legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledgment
Determine if the debt is acknowledged as qualified (e.g., 'one thousand dirhams qualified by a document') or indefinite (e.g., 'one thousand dirhams' without qualification).
A qualified acknowledgment acts like a definite reference to a specific debt.
2
Apply the repetition rule
If qualified and repeated twice in separate gatherings, only one thousand is owed because the qualification ties it to the same debt. If indefinite and repeated, two thousand are owed because each indefinite refers to a different debt.
This follows from the rule: indefinite repeated as indefinite = different; definite/qualified repeated = same.
3
Consider the gathering
If the acknowledgment occurs in the same gathering, it is considered a single acknowledgment regardless of definiteness.
The text states 'unless the gathering is the same' (illa an yattahida al-majlis).
4
Note the absence of riwaya
The text notes that for the case of acknowledging qualified first, then indefinite later in a different gathering, there is no transmitted report (la riwaya), but it deduces that two thousand are owed according to Abu Hanifa.
This shows how usul principles can fill gaps when explicit narrations are absent.
5
Summarize the four rational categories
The text outlines four possibilities based on whether the first and second mentions are definite or indefinite. For example, indefinite then definite: same entity; indefinite then indefinite: different; definite then definite: same; definite then indefinite: different.
These categories are illustrated with Quranic examples and the iqrar case.

Source Text

( وَيَثْبُتُ بِهَا وَاحِدٌ مَجْهولٌ عِندَ السَّامِعِ إِذَا كَانَتْ فِي الْأَخْبَارِ نَحْوُ رَأَيْت رَجُلًا فَإِذَا أُعِيدَتْ نَكِرَةً كَانَتْ غَيْرَ الْأُولَى ، وَإِذَا أُعِيدَتْ مَعْرِفَةٌ كَانَتْ عَيْنَهَا لِأَنَّ الْأَصْلَ فِي اللَّامِ الْعَهْدُ ، وَالْمَعْرِفَهُ إِذَا أُعِيدَتْ فَكَذَلِكَ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ) أَى إِذَا أُعِيدَتُ الْمَعْرِفَةُ نَكِرَةً كَانَ الثَّانِي غَيْرَ الْأَوَّلِ ، وَإِنْ أُعِيدَتْ مَعْرِفَةٌ كَانَ الثَّانِي عَيْنَ الْأَوَّلِ فَالْمُعْتَبَرَ نَكِيرُ الثَّانِي ، وَتَعْرِيفُهُ . ( وَقَالَ ابْنُ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُ فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى ) فَإِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا) لَنْ يَغْلِبَ عُسُرٌ يُسْرَيْنَ ، وَالْأَصَحُ أَنَّ هَذَا تَأْكِيدٌ ، وَإِنْ أَقَرَّ بِأَلْفِ مُقَيَّدٍ بِصَكَ مَرَّتَيْنِ يَجِبُ أَلْفٌ وَإِنْ أَقَرَّ بِهِ مُنَكَرًا يَجِبُ أَلْفَانِ عِنْدَهُ) أَيْ عِندَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ . ( إِلَّا أَنْ يَتَّحِدَ الْمَجْلِسُ ) فَالْأَقْسَامُ الْعَقْلِيَّةُ أَرْبَعَةٌ فَفِي قَوْله تَعَالَى : كَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ رَسُولًا فَعَصَى فِرْعَوْنُ الرَّسُولَ ) أُعِيدَتْ النَّكِرَةُ مَعْرِفَةٌ ، وَفِي قَوْله تَعَالَى ) إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا) أُعِيدَتُ النَّكِرَةُ نَكِرَةً ، وَالْمَعْرِفَةُ مَعْرِفَةٌ ، وَنَظِيرُ الْمَعْرِفَةِ الَّتِى تُعَادُ نَكِرَةً غَيْرُ مَذْكُورٍ وَهُوَ مَا إِذَا أَقَرٌ بِأَلْفِ مُقَيَّدٍ بِصَكَ ثُمَّ أَقَرَّ فِي مَجْلِسِ آخَرَ بِأَلْفِ مُنَكَرٍ لَا رِوَايَةَ لِهَذَا ، وَلَكِنْ يَنْبَغِي أَنْ يَجِبَ أَلْفَانِ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى .

ترجمہ: اور نکرہ ان جگہوں کے علاوہ میں خاص ہوتا ہے بوجہ ہونے اس کے دلالت کرنے والا فرد پر لیکن وہ ہوتا ہے مطلق جب کہ ہو وہ انشاء میں ) جیسے ان تذبحوا بقرة ( اور ثابت ہوتا ہے اس کے ساتھ واحد مجہول سامع کے نزدیک جبکہ ہو وہ اخبار میں جیسے رائیت رجلا ۔ پس جب اعادہ کیا جائے نکرہ کا تو ہوگا وہ اول کا غیر ۔ اور جب اعادہ کیا جائے اس نکرہ کا معرفہ تو ہوگا وہ اس نکرہ کا عین کیونکہ اصل لام میں عہد اور معرفہ کہ جب اعادہ کیا جائے اس کا تو اسی طرح ہوتا ہے دونوں صورتوں میں )

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a debt or right, which creates a legal obligation.
مقيد بصك (muqayyad bi-sakk): Qualified by a document; the acknowledgment is tied to a specific written evidence.
مجلس (majlis): A sitting or gathering; used to determine if separate acknowledgments are distinct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of iqrar, if someone acknowledges '1000 dirhams' in two separate gatherings, how much is obligated?
A) 1000 dirhams.
B) 2000 dirhams.
C) 3000 dirhams.
D) Depends on whether it is qualified.

Key Takeaway
In iqrar, repeating an indefinite acknowledgment in separate gatherings doubles the obligation; qualified or same-gathering acknowledgments do not.

Rules of Nakira and Ma'rifa in Repetition and Acknowledgment
شرح قواعد النکرة والمعرفة وأحکام الإعادة والإقرار

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a nakira and ma'rifa in Arabic legal texts and apply the Hanafi rules for their repetition to determine whether the second refers to the same or a different entity, and derive the fiqh ruling for iqrar cases.

Core Idea
The definiteness or indefiniteness of a repeated noun determines whether it refers to the same or a different entity in Hanafi legal interpretation.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the linguistic categories of nakira (indefinite) and ma'rifa (definite) carry specific legal consequences. This lesson unpacks the rules governing their usage in commands (insha') and statements (ikhbar), and critically, what happens when a noun is repeated. The passage from At-Tawdih wa At-Talwih presents these rules and applies them to a practical fiqh scenario of debt acknowledgment (iqrar), demonstrating how usul principles directly shape fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish insha' and ikhbar for nakira
Understand that in a command (insha'), nakira is absolute (mutlaq), requiring any instance. In a statement (ikhbar), nakira indicates a specific but unknown individual.
Example: 'Slaughter a cow' (insha') vs 'I saw a man' (ikhbar).
2
Apply repetition rule for nakira
If nakira is repeated as nakira, it refers to a different entity. If repeated as ma'rifa, it refers to the same entity because the definite article indicates prior mention (al-ahd).
Key: the second occurrence's definiteness determines reference.
3
Apply the same rule for ma'rifa
If ma'rifa is repeated as ma'rifa, it is the same entity. If repeated as nakira, it is a different entity. The decisive factor is the second noun's definiteness.
No explicit Quranic example given, but analogized from iqrar case.
4
Analyze the Quranic example
In Surah al-Inshirah (94:5-6), 'فإن مع العسر يسرا' is repeated. Ibn Abbas interpreted two different eases; the Hanafi preferred view (al-asahh) is that it is emphasis, not two different eases. Note the difference between tafsir and usul rule.
The usul rule remains: nakira repeated as nakira indicates different entities, but this verse may be a special case of emphasis.
5
Apply to iqrar case
If a person acknowledges a debt restricted by a check (muqayyad = definite) twice in separate sessions, only one debt is due. If the second acknowledgment is unrestricted (munkar = indefinite) in a different session, two debts are due according to Abu Hanifa.
This follows the rule: definite repeated as indefinite refers to a different entity.

والنكرة في غير هذه المواضع خاص لكنها تكون مطلقة إذا كانت في الإنشاء نحو قوله تعالى إن الله يأمركم أن تذبحوا بقرة. ويثبت بها واحد مجهول عند السامع إذا كانت في الأخبار نحو رأيت رجلا. فإذا أعيدت نكرة كانت غير الأولى، وإذا أعيدت معرفة كانت عينها لأن الأصل في اللام العهد. والمعرفة إذا أعيدت فكذلك في الوجهين) أي إذا أعيدت المعرفة نكرة كان الثاني غير الأول، وإن أعيدت معرفة كان الثاني عين الأول فالمعتبر نكير الثاني، وتعريفه. ترجمہ: اور نکرہ ان جگہوں کے علاوہ میں خاص ہوتا ہے بوجہ ہونے اس کے دلالت کرنے والا فرد پر لیکن وہ ہوتا ہے مطلق جب کہ ہو وہ انشاء میں ) جیسے ان تذبحوا بقرة ( اور ثابت ہوتا ہے اس کے ساتھ واحد مجهول سامع کے نزدیک جبکہ ہو وہ اخبار میں جیسے رائیت رجلا ۔ پس جب اعادہ کیا جائے نکرہ کا تو ہوگا وہ اول کا غیر ۔ اور جب اعادہ کیا جائے اس نکرہ کا معرفہ تو ہوگا وہ اس نکرہ کا عین کیونکہ اصل لام میں عہد اور معرفہ کہ جب اعادہ کیا جائے اس کا تو اسی طرح ہوتا ہے دونوں صورتوں میں ) یعنی جب اعادہ کیا جائے معرفہ کا نکرہ ہو گا ثانی اول کا غیر اور اگر اعادہ کیا جائے معرفہ کا تو ہو گا ثانی اول کا عین پس معتبر ثانی کا نکرہ ہونا ہے اور اس کا معرفہ ہوتا ہے ( ابن عباس فرماتے ہیں اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول فان مع العسر یسرا الا یہ کے بارہ میں یہ ہرگز نہیں غالب آ سکتی ایک سختی دو آسانیوں پر اور زیادہ صحیح بات یہ ہے کہ یہ تاکید ہے اور اگر اقرار کیا ایک شخص نے ایسے ہزار کا جو مقید تھا چیک کے ساتھ دو مرتبہ تو واجب ہوگا ایک ہزار اور اگر اقرار کیا عمرہ کے طور پر تو واجب ہو نگے دو ہزار امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک مگریہ کہ متحد ہو مجلس ) پس اقسام عقلی طور پر چار ہیں پس اللہ تعالٰی کے قول كَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا إِلَى فِرْعَوْنَ رَسُولاً فَعَصَى فِرْعَوْنَ الرَّسُولَ اعادہ کیا گیا ہے نکرہ کا معرفہ کے ساتھ اور اللہ تعالیٰ کے قول فان مع العسر يسرا ان مع العسر يسرا میں اعادہ کیا گیا ہے نکرہ کا نکرہ اور معرفہ کا معرفہ کے ساتھ اور نظیر اس معرفہ کی جسکا اعادہ کیا جائے نکرہ کے ساتھ مذکور نہیں ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ جب اقرار کرے وہ ایک ایسے ہزار کا جو مقید ہو چیک کے ساتھ پھر اقرار کرے دوسری مجلس میں ایک ایسے ہزار کا جو نکرہ ہو یعنی غیر مقید ہو ۔ اس کی روایت صراحہ تو نہیں ملتی لیکن مناسب یہ ہے کہ واجب ہوں گے دو ہزار امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ۔
And the indefinite noun in other than these contexts is specific, but it is absolute when it occurs in a command like His statement: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to slaughter a cow.' And it establishes a single unknown [entity] for the listener when it occurs in statements like 'I saw a man.' So when it is repeated as indefinite, it is other than the first; and when it is repeated as definite, it is the same as the first because the original in the definite article is for prior mention. And the definite noun when repeated is likewise in both aspects: i.e., if the definite is repeated as indefinite, the second is other than the first; and if it is repeated as definite, the second is the same as the first. So what matters is the indefiniteness or definiteness of the second. Ibn Abbas (may Allah be pleased with him) said regarding His statement: 'For indeed, with hardship [comes] ease. Indeed, with hardship [comes] ease' that one hardship will not overcome two eases, and the more correct [view] is that this is emphasis. And if someone acknowledges a thousand restricted by a check twice, one thousand is due; and if he acknowledges it as indefinite, two thousand are due according to him (i.e., Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him) unless the session is the same. So the rational divisions are four: in His statement 'As We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, but Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' the indefinite is repeated as definite; and in His statement 'Indeed, with hardship [comes] ease. Indeed, with hardship [comes] ease' the indefinite is repeated as indefinite, and the definite as definite. An example of a definite that is repeated as indefinite is not mentioned, and it is the case where one acknowledges a thousand restricted by a check, then acknowledges in another session a thousand as indefinite. There is no recorded transmission for this, but it should be that two thousand are due according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah Almighty have mercy on him. Translation: And the indefinite noun in other than these contexts is specific because it indicates an individual, but it is absolute when it occurs in a command, like 'slaughter a cow'. And it establishes a single unknown [entity] for the listener when it occurs in statements like 'I saw a man'. So when the indefinite is repeated, it will be other than the first; and when that indefinite is repeated as definite, it will be the same as that indefinite because the original in the definite article is for prior mention. And the definite noun when repeated is likewise in both cases: i.e., if the definite is repeated as indefinite, the second will be other than the first; and if the definite is repeated as definite, the second will be the same as the first. So what matters is the indefiniteness and definiteness of the second. Ibn Abbas says regarding Allah's statement 'For indeed, with hardship [comes] ease. Indeed, with hardship [comes] ease' that one hardship will never overcome two eases, and the more correct view is that this is emphasis. And if a person acknowledges a thousand that is restricted by a check twice, one thousand is due; and if he acknowledges it as indefinite (i.e., unrestricted), then two thousand are due according to Imam Abu Hanifa, unless the session is the same. So the rational divisions are four: in Allah's statement 'As We sent to Pharaoh a messenger, but Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' the indefinite is repeated as definite; and in Allah's statement 'Indeed, with hardship [comes] ease. Indeed, with hardship [comes] ease' the indefinite is repeated as indefinite, and the definite as definite. An example of a definite that is repeated as indefinite is not mentioned, and it is the case where one acknowledges a thousand restricted by a check, then acknowledges in another session a thousand as indefinite. There is no explicit transmission for this, but it is appropriate that two thousand are due according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage, from the Hanafi usul commentary At-Tawdih wa At-Talwih, presents several interrelated rules about the usage of nakira (indefinite) and ma'rifa (definite) nouns in legal contexts, with specific applications from fiqh. The passage is a parsing exercise that demonstrates how these linguistic categories affect legal interpretation.

First, the text establishes the general rule: nakira outside the previously mentioned contexts (like negative, conditional, or qualified by an adjective) is specific (خص) — it refers to a single, particular individual. However, when nakira occurs in a command (insha'), it remains absolute (مطلق), meaning it is not restricted by any further qualification. The example given is from the Quranic verse 'إن الله يأمركم أن تذبحوا بقرة' (Indeed, Allah commands you to slaughter a cow). Here, 'بقرة' (a cow) is nakira in an insha' context, so it demands slaughtering any cow without specification.

Second, when nakira occurs in a statement (ikhbar), it indicates a single entity that is unknown to the listener. For instance, 'رأيت رجلاً' (I saw a man) refers to one man, but the listener does not know which man.

Third, the passage discusses the repetition of nouns. If a nakira is repeated as nakira, the second refers to a different individual from the first. If a nakira is repeated as ma'rifa (with the definite article), the second refers to the same individual. This is because the definite article (ال) in its primary usage indicates prior mention (العهد). Similarly, if a ma'rifa is repeated, the same rule applies: if repeated as nakira, it is a different entity; if repeated as ma'rifa, it is the same entity. The decisive factor is the definiteness or indefiniteness of the second occurrence.

To illustrate, the passage mentions the Quranic verse 'فإن مع العسر يسرا' repeated twice. Ibn Abbas interpreted the repetition as meaning one hardship cannot overcome two eases, implying that 'يسر' (ease) in the second occurrence is a different ease from the first (i.e., indefinites repeated as indefinites indicate two different eases). However, the more correct view in Hanafi usul is that this is merely emphasis (تأكيد), not that the second ease is different. This reflects a nuance in tafsir but does not change the general rule.

Fourth, the text applies these rules to a fiqh example involving iqrar (acknowledgment of debt). If a person acknowledges (i.e., declares) that he owes a thousand dirhams restricted by a check (i.e., a specific debt) twice in two separate sessions, only one thousand is due because the second acknowledgment refers to the same debt (مقيد بصك = definite). But if he acknowledges a thousand in an unrestricted (indefinite) manner after a restricted acknowledgment in a different session, two thousand are due according to Imam Abu Hanifa, because the indefinite acknowledgment refers to a different debt. However, if both acknowledgments are in the same session, they are considered one, and only the first obligation stands.

The text then outlines the four rational possibilities based on definiteness/indefiniteness of the first and second occurrences: (1) nakira repeated as ma'rifa (Quranic example: 'رسولاً' then 'الرسول'), (2) nakira repeated as nakira (the 'عسر يسرا' verse), (3) ma'rifa repeated as ma'rifa (also in the 'عسر يسرا' verse if we consider 'اليسر' but the text uses 'يسر' without ال? Actually the second 'يسرا' is also nakira, so this is an example of nakira repeated as nakira, not ma'rifa; the text says 'والمعرفة معرفة' meaning that if a ma'rifa were repeated as ma'rifa it would be the same, but no example is given except the implicit rule), and (4) ma'rifa repeated as nakira (no explicit Quranic example, but analogized from the iqrar case: the first acknowledgment is definite (مقيد بصك), the second is indefinite (منكر), so the second refers to a different debt).

The passage concludes that for the fourth case (ma'rifa repeated as nakira), although there is no explicit transmitted ruling, it is appropriate (ينبغي) that two thousand are due according to Abu Hanifa, meaning the second acknowledgment is a separate obligation.

This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of deriving fiqh rulings from linguistic principles, particularly the usage of nakira and ma'rifa in Arabic. The ruling about iqrar is a clear example of how the definiteness or indefiniteness of a noun affects legal obligations.

Key Terms
نَكِرَة (nakira): An indefinite noun that in contexts other than negation, condition, or qualification indicates a specific individual; in insha' it is mutlaq; in ikhbar it indicates a single unknown entity.
مَعْرِفَة (ma'rifa): A definite noun, typically with the definite article al-, which when repeated indicates the same entity due to prior mention (al-ahd).
مُقَيَّد (muqayyad): A noun restricted by a condition or specification, here a debt restricted by a check, making it definite in reference.
مُنْكَر (munkar): An indefinite noun (nakira) in the context of iqrar, meaning an unrestricted acknowledgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, if a person acknowledges a thousand dirhams restricted by a check in one session, and then in a separate session acknowledges a thousand dirhams without any restriction (indefinite), how much is due according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) One thousand dirhams because the second acknowledgment is a repetition of the first.
B) Two thousand dirhams because the second acknowledgment is indefinite and refers to a different debt.
C) One thousand dirhams because both are considered the same debt if the amount is the same.
D) Two thousand dirhams only if the first acknowledgment was also indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The definiteness or indefiniteness of the second noun in a repetition determines whether it refers to the same or a different entity, which directly impacts fiqh rulings like debt acknowledgment.

Nakira in Insha vs Ikhbar: Legal Effect
usul rule: effect of indefinite noun context

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a nakira in a given legal text is in insha or ikhbar and state the resulting legal effect (specific but absolute vs. unknown individual).

Core Idea
An indefinite noun in a command is specific but absolute; in a statement it indicates an unknown individual.

In Hanafi usul, the interpretation of indefinite nouns is not uniform; it depends on the speech act. This lesson examines two key contexts: commands (insha) and statements (ikhbar), clarifying the legal consequence of each. Understanding this distinction prevents misapplication of generality in legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Determine the speech act
Is the nakira in a command/prohibition (insha) or a statement/report (ikhbar)? This is the primary context.
Insha includes commands, prohibitions, oaths; ikhbar includes news, descriptions, affirmations.
2
If insha: apply specificity and absoluteness
A nakira in insha is specific (khass) but absolute (mutlaq). It refers to one instance without any restriction.
Example: 'slaughter a cow' – one cow, any cow.
3
If ikhbar: apply unknown individual
A nakira in ikhbar indicates one individual that is known to the speaker but unknown to the listener.
Example: 'I saw a man' – one specific man from the speaker's perspective, unknown to listener.
4
Test with textual examples
Apply the rule to Quranic verses and everyday statements to solidify understanding.
Practice with various Quranic commands and prophetic reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نکرہ کے انشاء یا اخبار میں واقع ہونے کا حکم
تشریح:۔ یہ جو مواقع اور مقامات ذکر کیے گئے ہیں کہ نکرہ تحت الھی یا تحت الشرط واقع ہو یا موصوفہ بصفہ عام ہوتو ان کا حکم بیان کیا کہ ان صورتوں میں نکرہ عام ہوتا ہے اب فرماتے ہیں کہ ان مقامات کے علاوہ اگر نگرہ آجائے تو دیکھا جائے گا کہ یہ نکرہ انشاء میں ہے یا اخبار میں اگر انشاء میں ہو تو یہ نکرہ خاص ہوگا لیکن مطلق ہو گا کسی قید کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہوگا جیسے اللہ کا فرمان ہے ان اللہ یا مرکم ان تذبحو بقرۃ یہاں ”بقرہ نکرہ ہے اور انشاء میں ہے اس لیے اس سے مراد ایک گائے ہے لیکن کسی قید کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہے یعنی یہ حکم نبی اسرائیل کو تھا اور حکم مطلق گائے کو ذبح کرنے کا تھا تو وہ کوئی بھی گائے ذبح کر دیتے تو حکم پورا ہو جاتا یہ علیحدہ بات ہے کہ انہوں نے خود بار بار پوچھ پوچھ کر اپنے اوپر قیدیں لگوائیں۔ اور اگر نگرہ اخبار میں ہو تو اس سے ایک فرد ثابت ہو جائے گا اور وہ فرد سامع کے اعتبار سے مجہول ہوگا جیسے "رایت رجلاً " یہاں رجلا نکرہ ہے اور اخبار میں واقع ہے اسی لیے اس سے ایک فرد مراد ہے اور یہ فرداگر چہ متکام کے ہاں تو متعین ہی ہے کہ اس کو علم ہے کہ اس نے کسی آدمی کو دیکھا ہے لیکن سامع کے ہاں یہ فرد مجہول ہے۔
Explanation: These positions and places that were mentioned, that the indefinite noun occurs under negation or under condition or qualified by a general attribute, their ruling was stated that in these forms the indefinite becomes general. Now he says: Apart from these positions, if an indefinite appears, it will be seen whether this indefinite is in a command (insha) or in a statement (ikhbar). If it is in a command, then this indefinite is specific but absolute, not restricted by any restriction, like Allah's saying: 'Indeed Allah commands you to slaughter a cow' (2:67). Here 'cow' is indefinite and is in a command, so by it is meant one cow, but not restricted by any restriction, meaning that this command was to the Children of Israel and the command was to slaughter an absolute cow, so if they slaughtered any cow, the command would have been fulfilled. It is a separate matter that they themselves repeatedly asked and imposed restrictions upon themselves. And if the indefinite is in a statement, then one individual is established by it, and that individual is unknown from the perspective of the listener, like 'I saw a man'. Here 'a man' is indefinite and occurs in a statement, therefore by it is meant one individual, and this individual, although from the perspective of the speaker is specific because he knows whom he saw, but from the perspective of the listener this individual is unknown.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Hanafi usul about how to interpret indefinite nouns (nakira) depending on whether they occur in an insha (command/prohibition) or ikhbar (statement). The author explains that outside the previously mentioned contexts (like after negation or condition), a nakira in insha is specific (khass) but absolute (mutlaq) — it refers to a single instance without any restriction. The Quranic example of the cow in Surah al-Baqarah illustrates this: the command was to slaughter a cow, not a specific one, so any cow would suffice. In contrast, a nakira in ikhbar indicates one unknown individual from the listener's perspective, as in 'I saw a man'. The speaker knows the specific man, but the listener does not. This distinction is crucial for legal interpretation: commands with indefinite objects are not automatically general but require a single performance unless context indicates otherwise.

Key Terms
نکرہ (nakira): An indefinite noun; in usul al-fiqh, its scope depends on context: in insha it is specific but absolute, in ikhbar it indicates an unknown individual.
انشاء (insha): Speech that creates a legal effect, such as commands, prohibitions, and oaths.
اخبار (ikhbar): Speech that reports a fact, such as news or statements of past events.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if Allah commands 'slaughter a cow' (as in Quran 2:67), how many cows must be slaughtered to fulfill the command?
A) An indefinite number, as 'a cow' is general.
B) One cow, but it must be a specific cow described by the people.
C) One cow, and any cow is sufficient because the command is absolute.
D) Two cows, because the indefinite implies minimum two.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an indefinite noun in a command is specific but absolute (one instance, no restriction), while in a statement it indicates an unknown individual.

The Rule of Repeating Indefinite and Definite Nouns in Hanafi Usul
Explaining the effect of repetition of indefinite/definite nouns in statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that repeating an indefinite noun as indefinite indicates a different individual, and repeating a definite noun as definite indicates the same individual, and identify the dispute regarding the interpretation of Quran 94:5-6.

Core Idea
When a noun is repeated, its form (indefinite/definite) determines whether it refers to the same or a different entity.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: how the repetition of nouns in statements (ikhbar) affects their reference. Understanding this rule is crucial for interpreting Quranic verses and legal texts. We will examine the rule, its application in the Quran, and a famous dispute between Ibn Abbas and other scholars regarding the repetition in Surah al-Inshirah.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for repeating an indefinite noun
Recall that if an indefinite noun (nakira) is repeated as an indefinite, it refers to a different individual; if repeated as a definite, it refers to the same individual.
The key is the form of the second occurrence, not the first.
2
State the rule for repeating a definite noun
Similarly, if a definite noun (marifa) is repeated as a definite, it refers to the same individual; if repeated as an indefinite, it refers to a different individual.
The lam of definiteness (ال) indicates prior reference (ahd).
3
Apply the rule to Quran 94:5-6
Identify 'al-'usr' (definite) repeated as definite → same hardship. Identify 'yusra' (indefinite) repeated as indefinite → a different ease. Thus one hardship and two eases.
Ibn Abbas used this interpretation.
4
Analyze the dispute about tawkid
Some scholars consider the second verse as mere confirmation (tawkid), meaning both 'yusra' refer to the same ease. The matn prefers this view as 'asahh' (more correct). Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
The disagreement affects the meaning of the verse: one hardship vs. two eases.
5
Examine additional examples
Review the example of 'rasulan' (indefinite) then 'al-rasul' (definite) in Quran 73:15-16 → both refer to the same messenger. Also consider the fiqh example of iqrar (acknowledgment) to see the rule in action.
These examples confirm the rule.

نکرہ اور معرفہ کے اعادہ کا حکم
فاذا اعيدت نكرة كانت غیر الاولی:۔ یہاں سے ماتن اسی نکرہ کے بارے میں مزید تفصیل کر رہے ہیں جو اخبار میں واقع ہو اور اس سے مراد ایک فرد مجہول ہو ۔ کہ اگر اسی نکرہ کو دوبارہ نکرہ کی صورت میں ذکر کیا جائے ۔ تو اس سے مراد پہلے نکرہ کے علاوہ کوئی اور فرد ہوگا ۔ اور اگر اس کو معرفہ کی صورت میں ذکر کیا جائے تو اس سے مراد بعینہ پہلے والا فرد ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ لام تعریف میں اصل عہد ہے اور یہاں اس پر محمول کرنا ممکن ہے کیونکہ پہلے یہ معہود ہے لھذا اس کو عہد پر محمول کیا جائے گا اور اس سے پہلے والا فرد مراد ہوگا۔ اور اگر پہلے ایک لفظ کو معرفہ ذکر کیا جائے تو اس کا حکم بھی دونوں صورتوں میں یہی ہے یعنی اگر دوبارہ اس کو ٹکرہ ذکر کیا جائے تو اس سے پہلے فرد کے علاوہ کوئی اور فرد مراد ہوگا اور اگر اس کو معرفہ ذکر کیا جائے تو اس سے بعینہ پہلے والا فرد مراد ہو گا گویا معلوم ہوا کہ اصل اعتبار دوبارہ لوٹانے کا ہے کہ دوبارہ اگر نکرہ مذکور ہو تو اس سے مراد پہلے والے کا غیر ہو گا اور اگر دوبارہ معرفہ مذکور ہو تو اس سے پہلے والا بعینہ مراد ہو گا پہلے والا چاہے نکرہ ہو یا معرفہ ۔ قال ابن عباس : ۔ یہاں سے مذکورہ بالا صورتوں کی مثالیں دے رہے ہیں اگر کرہ کو دوبارہ نکرہ ذکر کیا جائے یا معرفہ کو دوباره معرفہ کو ذکر کیا جائے تو ان دونوں کی مثال فان مع العسر يسرا ان مع العسر یسرا۔ یہاں العسر معرفہ کو دوبارہ بھی معرفہ کی صورت میں ذکر کیا گیا اس لیے ثانی عصر سے مراد بھی اول عمر ہے یعنی دونوں سے ایک ہی عسر مراد ہے اور یسرا نکرہ کو دوبارہ نکرہ ذکر کیا گیا ہے تو ثانی "سر" سے اول "یر" کا غیر مراد ہے یعنی دونوں سے الگ الگ یسر مراد ہیں۔ یہی توجیہ ہے کہ حضرت ابن عباس نے اس آیت کو لیکر فرمایا کہ ایک عسر دو یسر پر غالب نہیں آسکتی۔ والا صبح ان هذا تاکید : ۔ اس آیت میں بعض حضرات نے یہ فرمایا ہے کہ یہاں ثانی آیت اول آیت کی تاکید ہے اور عسر اور یسر دونوں سے ایک ہی عسر یا سیسر مراد ہے ،اتن نے اسی قول کو اصبح قرار دیا ہے۔
اور اگر یکرہ کو دوبارہ معرفہ کی صورت میں ذکر کیا جائے تو اس کی مثال جیسے قرآن پاک میں ہے " كما ارسلنا الی فرعون رسولا یہاں رسول نکرہ ہے اور اس سے مراد حضرت موسیٰ ہیں اور اس کے بعد ہے فصی فرعون الرسول یہاں رسول کو معرفہ بنا کر ذکر کیا گیا ہے اور اس سے مراد بھی حضرت موسیٰ ہیں ۔ تو معلوم ہوا کہ اگر نمرہ کو دوبارہ معرفہ ذکر کیا جائے تو دونوں سے ایک ہی مراد ہوگا۔
اور اگر معرفہ کو ٹکرہ بنا کر لوٹایا جائے تو اس کی مثال اصول فخر الاسلام میں مذکور نہیں ہے البتہ ایک فقہی مسئلہ اس کی مثال بن سکتا ہے۔ کوئی آدمی اقرار کرے اقر بالف مقید ۔۔ یعنی اس ہزار کا اقرار کرے جو چیک میں ہوں یہاں "الف" معرفہ ہے کہ مطلق ہزار کا اقرار نہیں کر رہا بلکہ چیک میں مقید ہزار کا اقرار کر رہا ہے۔ اس کے بعد دوبارہ اگر وہ ہزار کا اقرار کرتا ہے دوسری مجلس میں لیکن اس ہزار کو مطلقا ذکر کرتا ہے۔ تو اس سے مراد پہلے والے ہزار کے علاوہ کوئی اور ہزار مراد ہو گا لھذا امام
The Rule of Repeating Indefinite and Definite Nouns: So when an indefinite noun is repeated, it is other than the first. From here, the matn is giving more detail about the same indefinite noun that occurs in a statement and means an unknown individual. That if that same indefinite noun is mentioned a second time in the form of an indefinite, then by it is meant some other individual besides the first indefinite. And if it is mentioned in the form of a definite, then by it is meant exactly the first individual, because the lam of definiteness fundamentally indicates prior reference (ahd), and here it is possible to apply it because the first is known, so it will be applied to prior reference and the first individual is meant. And if a word is first mentioned as a definite, its ruling is also the same in both forms: if it is repeated as an indefinite, then by it is meant an individual other than the first; if it is repeated as a definite, then by it is meant exactly the first individual. So it is understood that the essential consideration is the repetition: if it is repeated as indefinite, it means other than the first; if repeated as definite, it means exactly the same as the first, whether the first was indefinite or definite. Ibn Abbas said: From here he gives examples of the above cases: if an indefinite is repeated as indefinite, or a definite is repeated as definite, their example is 'For indeed, with hardship [will be] ease. Indeed, with hardship [will be] ease' (94:5-6). Here 'al-usra' (the hardship) is definite and is repeated as definite, so the second 'usra' means the first 'usra' — both refer to the same hardship. And 'yusra' (ease) is indefinite and repeated as indefinite, so the second 'yusra' means other than the first 'yusra' — i.e., two distinct eases. This is the interpretation that Ibn Abbas gave regarding this verse, that one hardship cannot overcome two eases. 'And the more correct is that this is confirmation (tawkid)': In this verse, some scholars said that the second verse is a confirmation of the first verse, and both 'usra' and 'yusra' refer to the same hardship and ease. The matn declared this saying as the more correct. And if an indefinite is repeated in the form of a definite, its example is as in the Holy Quran: 'As We sent to Pharaoh a messenger' (73:15). Here 'rasulan' (a messenger) is indefinite and refers to Prophet Moses. And then it says 'But Pharaoh disobeyed the messenger' (73:16). Here 'al-rasul' (the messenger) is mentioned as definite and also refers to Prophet Moses. So it is understood that if an indefinite is repeated as definite, both refer to the same. And if a definite is repeated as an indefinite, its example is not mentioned in Usul Fakhr al-Islam, but a fiqh issue can serve as an example. A person acknowledges 'I acknowledge one thousand that is restricted' — i.e., he acknowledges one thousand that are in a cheque. Here 'alf' (one thousand) is definite because he is not acknowledging an absolute thousand but a thousand restricted to a cheque. Then later, if he acknowledges one thousand again in a second session but mentions that thousand absolutely (without restriction), then by it is meant a thousand other than the first thousand. Therefore, according to Imam Abu Hanifa...
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Hanafi usul regarding the interpretation of repeated nouns in statements (ikhbar). The author explains that if an indefinite noun (nakira) is repeated in the same indefinite form, it refers to a different individual (ghair) from the first. If it is repeated in the definite form (marifa), it refers to the same individual (ayn). The same rule applies if the first noun is definite: repeating it as indefinite indicates a different individual, repeating as definite indicates the same. The underlying principle is the 'lam of definiteness' (ال) indicates prior reference (ahd), meaning the definite noun points back to a previously mentioned entity. The Quranic example of 'inna ma'a al-'usri yusra' (94:5-6) illustrates both cases: 'al-'usr' (hardship) is definite and repeated as definite, so both refer to the same hardship; 'yusra' (ease) is indefinite and repeated as indefinite, so two different eases. This interpretation is attributed to Ibn Abbas, who concluded that one hardship cannot overcome two eases. However, some scholars consider the second verse as mere confirmation (tawkid), meaning both hardship and ease are the same. The author mentions that the matn considers this latter view 'asahh' (more correct). The passage also provides an example of indefinite repeated as definite from Quran 73:15-16 ('rasulan' then 'al-rasul'), and for definite repeated as indefinite, a fiqh example of an acknowledgment (iqrar) is given: if someone first acknowledges a restricted thousand (e.g., in a cheque) and then later acknowledges an absolute thousand, the second acknowledgment refers to a different thousand.

Key Terms
نکرہ (nakira): An indefinite noun; when repeated after an indefinite, it refers to a different individual.
معرفہ (marifa): A definite noun; when repeated after a definite, it refers to the same individual. Its lam indicates prior reference (ahd).
اعادہ (i'ada): Repetition of a noun; the key factor is the form (nakira or marifa) in the second occurrence.
تاکید (tawkid): Confirmation; a second occurrence that merely emphasizes the first without adding new meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the verse 'فإن مع العسر يسرا * إن مع العسر يسرا' (94:5-6), if we apply the rule that repeating a definite as definite indicates the same, and repeating an indefinite as indefinite indicates a different, what does Ibn Abbas conclude?
A) Both 'usra' and 'yusra' are the same; the verse is mere confirmation.
B) There is one hardship and two eases; one hardship cannot overcome two eases.
C) There is one hardship and one ease; the second verse is a separate promise.
D) There are two hardships and two eases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: repeating a definite noun as definite indicates the same; repeating an indefinite as indefinite indicates a different entity; this principle is crucial for interpreting repetitions in legal and Quranic texts.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
6 min
Generality of 'Ayy' (أي) When Qualified by a Description
Analyzing when 'ayy' yields generality vs. specific choice based on description attachment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate when 'ayy' indicates generality versus a single indefinite individual by analyzing the presence of a description and the possibility of choice.

Core Idea
'Ayy' becomes general only when qualified by a description that applies to each individual without choice.

The word 'ayy' (أي) is often assumed to always indicate generality, but Hanafi usul teaches that it is indefinite (nakirah) by itself. It only becomes general (ʿāmm) when followed by a description (ṣifah) that applies to every possible individual. The key is whether the description leaves room for choice by an agent. This lesson will parse the examples from the classical text to understand this precise rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default status of 'ayy'
Recognize that 'ayy' is a nakirah (indefinite) and does not indicate generality by itself.
This is the baseline before any description is added.
2
Recognize the role of the description (sifah)
When a description follows 'ayy', it can turn it into a general term if the description applies to every individual covered by the word.
The description must be such that it cannot be limited to one individual by choice.
3
Distinguish between two scenarios
Compare examples where the description is attached to the subject (active, no choice) vs. where the description involves an external agent who can choose (passive/imperative).
The first scenario yields generality; the second yields a single indefinite.
4
Apply to the given examples
Analyze 'أَيُّ عَبِيدِي ضَرَبَكَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ' (active, no choice) → all slaves who struck are freed. 'أَيُّ عَبِيدِى ضَرَبْتِه' (passive, choice) → only one is freed by the addressee's choice.
The text also gives examples of hide tanning and bread to reinforce the distinction.
5
Note the alternative explanation
The text offers a second distinction: 'ayy' only covers a single indefinite, but in the first case each slave is considered individually because the condition applies to each; in the second, only one is intended because choice is possible.
This reinforces the same conclusion from a different angle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا أَى ، وَهِيَ نَكِرَةٌ تَعُمُّ بِالصَّفَةِ فَإِنْ قَالَ أَيُّ عَبِيدِي ضَرَبَكَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَضَرَبُوهُ عَتَقُوا وَإِنْ قَالَ أَيُّ عَبِيدِى ضَرَبْتِه لَا يَعْتِقُ إِلَّا وَاحِدٌ قَالُوا لِاَنَّ فِي الْأَوَّلِ وَصَفَهُ بِالضَّرْبِ فَصَارَ عَامَّا بِهِ وَفِي الثَّانِي قَطَعَ الْوَصْفَ عَنْهُ وَهَذَا الْفَرْقُ مُشكِلٌ مِنْ جِهَةِ النَّحْوِ لأنَّ فِى الأَوَّلِ وَصَفَهُ بِالصَّارِبِيَّةِ وَفِي الثّانِي بِالمَضْرُوبِيَّةِ وَهُنَا فَرْقَ آخَرُ وَهُوَ أَنَّ أَيَّا لَا يَتَنَاوَلُ إِلَّا الْوَاحِدَ الْمُنَكَّرَ فَفِي الْأَوَّلِ فِى قَوْلِهِ أَيُّ عَبِيدِى ضَرَبَكَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ (لَمَا كَانَ عِتْقُهُ) أَيْ عِتْقُ الْوَاحِدِ المُنكَرِ مُعَلَّقًا بِضَرْبِهِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ الْغَيْرِ فَيُعْتَقُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ وَاحِدٌ مُفْرَدٌ فَحِينَئِذٍ لَا تَبْطُلُ الْوَحْدَةُ وَلَوْ لَمْ يَثْبُتُ هَذَا أَى عِتْقُ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ . (وَلَيْسَ الْبَعْضُ أَوْلَى مِنْ الْبَعْضِ لَبَطَلَ أَى الْكَلَامُ بِالْكُلِّيَّةِ وَفِي الثَّانِي وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ أَى عَبِيدِى ضَرَبُته يَثْبُتُ الْوَاحِدُ وَيَتَخَيْرُ فِيهِ الْفَاعِلُ) إِذْ هُنَاكَ يُمْكِنُ التَّخْيِيرُ مِنْ الْفَاعِلِ الْمُخَاطَبِ بِخِلَافِ الْأَوَّلِ نَحْوُ أَيُّمَا إهَابٍ دُبِعَ فَقَدْ طَهَّرَ هَذَا نَظِيرُ الْأَوَّلِ فَإِنْ طَهَارَتَهُ مُتَعَلَّقَةٌ بِدِبَاغَتِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُ فَاعِلٌ مُعَيَّنٌ يُمْكِنُ مِنْهُ التَّخْسِيرُ فَيَدُلُّ عَلَى الْعُمُومِ وَنَحْوُ كُل أَي خُبْزِ تُرِيدُ هَـذَا نَظِيرُ الثَّانِي فَإِنَّ التَّخْسِيرَ مِنْ الْفَاعِلِ الْمُخَاطَبِ مُمْكِنْ هُنَا فَلا يَتَمَكَّنُ مِنْ أكُلِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ بَلْ أَكُلُ وَاحِدٍ لَكِنْ
Among them is 'أي' (ayy). It is a nakirah that becomes general (taʿummu) by a description. If someone says, 'Any one of my slaves who struck you is free,' and they strike him, they all become free. But if he says, 'Any one of my slaves whom you struck' (i.e., with 'struck' in the passive), only one becomes free. They said: because in the first, he described the slave with the act of striking, so it became general by that description; in the second, he cut off the description from the slave (i.e., the description is on the speaker). This difference is problematic from a grammatical perspective, because in the first he described the slave with 'striking' (active), and in the second with 'being struck' (passive). There is another difference: 'ayy' does not encompass except a single indefinite individual. In the first statement, 'Any one of my slaves who struck you is free,' the manumission of that single indefinite slave is tied to his striking, regardless of others. Thus, each slave is freed individually, because each is a single unit; the unity is not invalidated. If this (manumission of each) were not established, and no individual were preferred over another, the entire statement would become void. In the second statement, 'Any one of my slaves whom you struck,' only one is established, and the doer (the speaker) has a choice (to choose which slave). Because there, choice is possible from the addressed doer, unlike the first. For example: 'Any hide that is tanned has become pure'—this is similar to the first, because its purification is tied to its tanning, without a specific doer from whom choice is possible, so it indicates generality. And the example: 'Any bread that you want'—this is similar to the second, because choice from the addressed doer is possible here, so he cannot eat every single one, but rather eats one, but...
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage discusses the word 'أي' (ayy) as a tool for generality in Hanafi usul. The author states that 'أي' is a nakirah (indefinite) that becomes general (عموم) when qualified by a description (صفة). Two examples are given from the context of slave manumission: if the master says 'Any slave who struck you (active) is free,' then if all slaves struck, all become free because the description applies to each. But if he says 'Any slave whom you struck (passive),' only one becomes free because the description is on the addressee's action, and a single slave is intended. The author notes a grammatical difficulty with this distinction (the first describes the slave as striker, the second as struck). Then a better distinction is provided: 'أي' only covers a single indefinite individual. In the first case, the manumission of each slave is tied to his own striking, so each is freed individually; if that were not so, the statement would be void. In the second case, only one slave is established and the speaker can choose which one, because choice is possible for the addressee. Two additional examples illustrate: 'Any hide that is tanned becomes pure'—this is like the first, as there is no specific agent to choose, so it indicates generality. 'Any bread you want'—like the second, because the addressee can choose, so he cannot eat every piece but only one. The passage ends abruptly at 'but' (لكن).

Key Terms
أي (ayy): A word that is indefinite by itself but becomes general when followed by a description.
صفة (sifah): A descriptive phrase that qualifies the noun, making the ruling apply to each individual.
عموم (ʿumūm): Generality; the ruling applies to all individuals covered by the term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does every slave who struck become free in the statement 'أَيُّ عَبِيدِي ضَرَبَكَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ'?
A) Because 'ayy' is general by itself
B) Because the active description 'ضرب' generalizes 'ayy'
C) Because the slave is indefinite
D) Because it is a conditional statement

Key Takeaway
The generality of 'ayy' depends on whether the description attaches to each individual without allowing choice; if choice is possible, only one is intended.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

The Generality of 'Ayy': When Description Makes the Word General
Analyzing the word 'أيّ' as a general word in conditional statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of 'ayy' to determine when it indicates generality (umum) and when it indicates a single indefinite entity, using the criterion of qualification by description and the possibility of choice.

Core Idea
'Ayy' becomes general only when qualified by a description; without a description, it refers to a single indefinite entity.

The word 'ayy' (أي) is one of the words of generality in Arabic, but Hanafi usul al-fiqh specifies a crucial condition: it only operates as a general term when accompanied by a description (sifah). This lesson dissects the two examples from the text — one where 'ayy' is qualified by the slave's action (hitting) and one where it is qualified by the addressee's action (being hit) — to extract the precise legal rule. You will see how the later Hanafi scholars refined the distinction based on whether the agent of the action has the ability to choose, which determines whether the statement indicates generality or merely a single occurrence with choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional statements
Locate the two contrasting examples in the text: (A) 'Any of my slaves who hit you is free' and (B) 'Any of my slaves you hit is free'. Note the difference in the grammatical subject of the verb.
2
State the rulings for each
In (A), if all slaves hit, all are freed. In (B), only one slave is freed, and the addressee may choose which one.
The ruling difference is the thamarah (practical consequence).
3
Extract the usul principle
From the rulings, the principle is: 'ayy' becomes a general word (عام) when it is qualified by a description (sifah) that specifies the attribute of the individuals. In (A), the description 'hit you' makes 'ayy' general. In (B), no such description of the slave's action is present.
The text also introduces a refined distinction: possibility of choice. In (B), the addressee can choose, which prevents generality.
4
Analyze the grammatical objection and its resolution
The text notes a grammatical problem: in both cases 'ayy' is qualified by a verb (hitting vs. being hit). The resolution is that the key factor is not the grammatical form but whether the agent of the action can choose. In (A) the agent is the slave who hits; there is no choice among slaves — all who hit are freed. In (B) the agent is the addressee; he can choose which slave he hit, so only one is freed.
This refinement is essential for applying the rule correctly.
5
Apply to analogical cases
The text gives two analogies: (1) 'Any skin that is tanned is purified' — analogous to (A) because no specific agent can choose; (2) 'Any bread you want' — analogous to (B) because the addressee chooses. Practice applying the rule to these.
These analogies reinforce the principle.

وَمِنْهَا أَى ، وَهِيَ نَكِرَةٌ تَعُمُّ بِالصَّفَةِ فَإِنْ قَالَ أَيُّ عَبِيدِي ضَرَبَكَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ فَضَرَبُوهُ عَتَقُوا وَإِنْ قَالَ أَيُّ عَبِيدِى ضَرَبْتِه لَا يَعْتِقُ إِلَّا وَاحِدٌ قَالُوا لِاَنَّ فِي الْأَوَّلِ وَصَفَهُ بِالضَّرْبِ فَصَارَ عَامَّا بِهِ وَفِي الثَّانِي قَطَعَ الْوَصْفَ عَنْهُ وَهَذَا الْفَرْقُ مُشكِلٌ مِنْ جِهَةِ النَّحْوِ لأنَّ فِى الأَوَّلِ وَصَفَهُ بِالصَّارِبِيَّةِ وَفِي الثّانِي بِالمَضْرُوبِيَّةِ وَهُنَا فَرْقَ آخَرُ وَهُوَ أَنَّ أَيَّا لَا يَتَنَاوَلُ إِلَّا الْوَاحِدَ الْمُنَكَّرَ فَفِي الْأَوَّلِ فِى قَوْلِهِ أَيُّ عَبِيدِى ضَرَبَكَ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ (لَمَا كَانَ عِتْقُهُ) أَيْ عِتْقُ الْوَاحِدِ المُنكَرِ مُعَلَّقًا بِضَرْبِهِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ الْغَيْرِ فَيُعْتَقُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ بِاعْتِبَارِ أَنَّهُ وَاحِدٌ مُفْرَدٌ فَحِينَئِذٍ لَا تَبْطُلُ الْوَحْدَةُ وَلَوْ لَمْ يَثْبُتُ هَذَا أَى عِتْقُ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ . (وَلَيْسَ الْبَعْضُ أَوْلَى مِنْ الْبَعْضِ لَبَطَلَ أَى الْكَلَامُ بِالْكُلِّيَّةِ وَفِي الثَّانِي وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُ أَى عَبِيدِى ضَرَبُته يَثْبُتُ الْوَاحِدُ وَيَتَخَيْرُ فِيهِ الْفَاعِلُ) إِذْ هُنَاكَ يُمْكِنُ التَّخْيِيرُ مِنْ الْفَاعِلِ الْمُخَاطَبِ بِخِلَافِ الْأَوَّلِ نَحْوُ أَيُمَا إهَابٍ دُبِعَ فَقَدْ طَهَّرَ هَذَا نَظِيرُ الْأَوَّلِ فَإِنْ طَهَارَتَهُ مُتَعَلَّقَةٌ بِدِبَاغَتِهِ مِنْ غَيْرِ أَنْ يَكُونَ لَهُ فَاعِلٌ مُعَيَّنٌ يُمْكِنُ مِنْهُ التَّخْسِيرُ فَيَدُلُّ عَلَى الْعُمُومِ وَنَحْوُ كُل أَي خُبْزِ تُرِيدُ هَـذَا نَظِيرُ الثَّانِي فَإِنَّ التَّخْسِيرَ مِنْ الْفَاعِلِ الْمُخَاطَبِ مُمْكِنْ هُنَا فَلا يَتَمَكَّنُ مِنْ أكُلِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ بَلْ أَكُلُ وَاحِدٍ لَكِنْ يَتَخَيَّرُ فِيهِ الْمُخَاطَبُ وَمِثْلُ هَذَا الْكَلامِ لِلتَّخْيِيرِ فِى الْعُرْفِ .
And among them is 'ayy', and it is a noun that becomes general by description. So if he said: 'Any of my slaves who hit you is free' and they all hit him, then all become free. And if he said: 'Any of my slaves you hit is free', only one is freed. They (jurists) said: because in the first he qualified it with hitting, so it became general thereby; and in the second he cut off the description from it. This distinction is problematic from a grammatical perspective, because in the first he qualified it with being a hitter, and in the second with being hit. And here is another distinction: that 'ayy' encompasses only the indefinite single. So in the first, in his statement 'Any of my slaves who hit you is free'—since his emancipation, i.e., the emancipation of the indefinite single, is suspended on his hitting, disregarding others, then each one is emancipated considering that he is a single individual; so then the singleness is not invalidated. And if this—i.e., the emancipation of each one—were not established (and no part is more deserving than another), then the statement would become entirely invalid. And in the second, i.e., his statement 'Any of my slaves you hit', the one is established and the agent (the person addressed) may choose among them. For there it is possible to give choice to the agent addressed, unlike the first. For example, 'Any skin that is tanned is purified'—this is analogous to the first, for its purification is attached to its tanning without having a specific agent from whom choice is possible, so it indicates generality. And for example, 'Any bread you want'—this is analogous to the second, for choice from the agent addressed is possible here, so he is not able to eat every one, but he can eat one, yet the addressee may choose among them. Such speech is used for giving choice in custom.
Source pages p. 620

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'أيّ' (ayy) as one of the words of generality (ألفاظ العموم) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author explains the ruling based on whether 'أيّ' is qualified by a description (صفة) or not. The key principle is: 'أيّ' is originally a noun that becomes general (عام) when accompanied by a description. 

The first example: if someone says 'أي عبيدي ضربك فهو حر' (Any of my slaves who hit you is free) and all slaves hit him, then all are freed. This is because the description 'ضربك' (hit you) qualifies 'أيّ', making it general, applying to every slave who performs the hitting. Each slave is considered individually, so if all hits, all are freed.

The second example: if the statement is 'أي عبيدي ضربته' (Any of my slaves you hit is free), only one slave becomes free, and the speaker (the person addressed) has the choice of which one. Here 'أيّ' is not qualified by a description of the slave's action; instead, it is the addressee's action that is specified. Since 'أيّ' only encompasses a single indefinite entity, and the addressee can choose which slave he hit, it does not imply generality.

The author then addresses a grammatical difficulty: in the first example, the slave is described as hitting (ضاربي), and in the second, as being hit (مضروبي). But the more precise distinction is the possibility of choice by the agent (فاعل) in the second case, which prevents generality. 

This is supported by two analogies: (1) 'أيما إهاب دبغ فقد طهر' (Any skin that is tanned is purified) – analogous to the first case because there is no specific agent giving choice, so it indicates generality; (2) 'كل أي خبز تريد' (Any bread you want) – analogous to the second case because the addressee can choose, so it indicates only one.

Key Terms
أيّ (ayy): A word of generality (عام) when qualified by a description; otherwise it specifies a single indefinite entity.
صِفَة (sifah): A description or attribute that, when attached to 'أيّ', makes it general and encompassing all individuals bearing that attribute.
عام (aam): A term that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction; here 'أيّ' becomes 'عام' by virtue of its description.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul analysis in this passage, when does 'أيّ' (ayy) function as a general word (عام)?
A) Whenever it is used in a sentence.
B) Only when it is qualified by a description (صفة).
C) Only when it is followed by a noun in the accusative case.
D) When it is repeated in a statement.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ayy' indicates generality only when qualified by a description that eliminates choice; otherwise it refers to a single indefinite entity with the possibility of choice.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
The Word 'Ayy' as a Word of 'Umum: Two Interpretations
Analyzing the word 'ayy' as a generalizing particle with attribute and without

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two interpretations of 'ayy' and apply the correct Hanafi distinction based on the distributive nature of 'ayy'.

Core Idea
Ayy includes one indefinite individual; generality arises when each qualifies independently, not from an attached attribute.

The word 'ayy' (أي) is one of the words of generality (alfaz al-'umum) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Its interpretation can drastically change legal outcomes in conditional statements. This lesson examines two conflicting interpretations of 'ayy' in the context of manumission declarations, refutes a flawed attribute-based reasoning, and establishes the correct distributive understanding that preserves the integrity of speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'ayy' as a word of 'umum
Recognize that 'ayy' is an indefinite noun that becomes general when annexed to a noun and followed by a general attribute (sifah). Its usage is with idafah.
The attribute must be general for 'ayy' to be general.
2
Examine two legal examples
Consider the two expressions: (1) 'ayy 'abidi darabak fa-huwa hurr' (any slave who struck you is free) and (2) 'ayy 'abidi darabtahu fa-huwa hurr' (any slave whom you struck is free). Note the different outcomes: all freed vs. only one freed.
The juristic ruling is agreed upon; the dispute is over the reasoning.
3
Understand the attribute-based difference
Some usulis argue that in the first expression, the attribute 'darabak' is attached to 'ayy' and is general, making 'ayy' general. In the second, the attribute is cut off and attached to the addressee, making 'ayy' specific.
This difference is grammatically problematic.
4
Analyze the Hanafi refutation
The author refutes the attribute-based difference: lexically both attributes are similar (striking vs. being struck); grammatically the clause after 'ayy' is not an attribute but part of a conditional or relative clause. Hence the difference is unsound.
The refutation uses both lexical and grammatical arguments.
5
Grasp the correct distributive difference
'Ayy' includes one indefinite individual without specification. In the first, each slave individually performs the striking, so each is the intended individual relative to his own act, leading to generality. In the second, the addressee's act is singular, so only one slave is freed.
This preserves the meaning of 'ayy' and avoids invalidating the speech.

الترويج لحل التوضيح والتلويح
يَتَخَيَّرُ فِيهِ الْمُخَاطَبُ وَمِثْلُ هَذَا الْكَلامِ لِلتَّخْيِيرِ فِى الْعُرْفِ .
ترجمہ:۔ (اور ان میں سے ایک اتی ہے اور نکرہ ہے عام ہوتا ہے صفت کی وجہ سے پس اگر اس نے کہا ای عبدی ضربک میرے جون سے غلام نے تجھے مارا پس وہ آزاد ہے۔ پس ان سب غلاموں نے اس کو مارا تو سب کے سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے اور اگر اس نے کہا ای عبدی ضربتہ فھو میرے جو نسے غلام کو تو مارے گا تو وہ آزاد ہے تو نہیں آزاد ہو گا مگر ایک۔ فقہا نے یہ قول کیا ہے اس لیے کہ پہلی صورت میں اس کی وصف ذکر کی ہے ضرب کیسا تھ تو وہ عام ہو گیا ہے اس وصف کی وجہ سے۔ اور دوسری صورت میں کاٹ دیا گیا ہے وصف کو اس سے۔ اور یہ فرق مشکل ہے نحو کی جہت سے کیونکہ پہلی صورت میں اس کا وصف ضاربت کیسا تھ ہے اور دوسری صورت میں مضروبیت کیسا تھ ہے۔ اور یہاں ایک دوسرا فرق ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ بیشک انی نہیں شامل ہوتا مگر ایک نمرہ کو پس صورت اول میں ) یعنی اس کے قول ای عبدی ضربک فھور میں (جب ہے اس کی آزادی ) یعنی واحد منکر کی آزادی معلق ہے اس کے مارنے کے ساتھ قطع نظر اس کے غیر سے پس آزاد ہو جائیگا ہر ایک اس اعتبار سے کہ وہ منفرد ہے پس اس وقت نہیں باطل ہوگی وحدت اور اگر نہ ثابت ہو یہ ) (یعنی ہر ایک کی آزادی ( اور نہیں ہیں بعض اولی بعض سے تو باطل ہو جائے گا) یعنی کلام ( کلی طور پر اور صورت پانی میں ) اور وہ اس کا قول اسی عبدی ضربتہ ہے ثابت ہوگا ایک اور اختیار ہو گا اس میں فاعل کو ) کیونکہ یہاں ممکن ہے اختیار دینا فاعل مخاطب سے بخلاف صورت اول کے جیسے ایما اصاب دبغ فقد طمر ) یہ نظیر ہے اول کی کیونکہ اس کی طہارت معلق ہے اس کی وباغت کے ساتھ بغیر اس کے کہ ہو اس کیلئے فاعل متعین کہ ممکن ہو اس سے اختیار دنیا پس یہ دلالت کرتا ہے عموم پر ( اور جیسے کل ای خبز ترید ) یہ نظیر ہے ثانی کی کیونکہ اختیار دینا فاعل مخاطب کو ممکن ہے یہاں پر نہیں ہے قادر وہ ہر ایک کو کھانے پر بلکہ ہر ایک کے کھانے پر قادر ہے لیکن اختیار ہو گا اس میں مخاطب کو اور اس کلام کی مثل اختیار دینے کیلئے آتی ہے عرف میں۔
الفاظ عموم میں سے لفظ امنی کا حکم
تشریح: ومنھا اتنی :۔ عام کے الفاظ میں سے ایک آتی ہے ۔ اسٹی" کی وضع تو ایک خاص فرد کے لئے ہے لیکن جب موصوف ہو اور اس کی صفت عام ہو تو یہ عام ہو جاتا ہے اور اس کا استعمال اضافت کے ساتھ ہوتا ہے اگر یہ نکرہ کی طرف مضاف ہو تو اس کا عام ہونا ظاہر ہے اور اگر معرفہ کی طرف مضاف ہو تو بھی اس کے افراد میں سے علی سبیل الہدلیت کوئی فرد مبہم مراد ہوگا۔ جیسے کوئی آدمی کہے ای عبیدی ضربک تصور " یہاں ای" کی صفت ضرب ہے جو کہ عام ہے۔ اس لیے "ای " عام ہے لھذا اگر تمام غلاموں نے مخاطب کو مارا تو تمام غلام آزاد ہو جائیں گے ۔ اور اگر یہ کہا ای عبیدی ضربتہ ھور اور مخاطب نے تمام غلاموں کو ماردیا تو پھر تمام غلام آزاد نہیں ہونگے صرف ایک غلام آزاد ہوگا اور اس کو اختیار کرنے میں مولی کو اختیار ہوگا۔ ان دونوں صورتوں میں فرق کیا ہے؟ باقی اصولی حضرات نے یہ فرق بیان کیا ہے اول صورت میں ای" کی صفت "ضرب" ہے جو غلاموں کی طرف نسبت کرتے ہوئے عام ہے اس لیے ای عام ہے۔ اور صورت ثانی میں اس صفت کو آئی سے منقطع کر دیا گیا ہے اور اس کی نسبت مخاطب کی طرف کر دی گئی ہے اس لیے وہ عام نہیں ہے۔ لیکن ماتن" نے اس فرق کو رد کیا ہے اور کہا ہے کہ صفت سے کیا مراد ہے؟ صفت لغوی یا نحوی اگر صفت لغوی مراد ہے تو یہ صفت دونوں صورتوں میں پائی جاتی ہے فرق صرف اتنا ہے کہ اول صورت میں صفت ضار بیت للمخاطب ہے اور ثانی صورت میں مضر و بیت للمخا طب ہے۔ تو دونوں میں صفت کے اعتبار سے کوئی فرق نہیں ہے اور اگر صفت سے مراد صفت نحوی ہے تو یہ صفت کسی صورت میں بھی نہیں پائی جاتی اور نہ اول میں نہ ثانی میں کیونکہ تمام نحاۃ کے نزدیک کلمہ آئی یا تو شرط ہے یا موصول ہے تو اس کے بعد والا جملہ یا تو اس کی جزاء ہے یا اس کا صلہ ہے صفت نہیں ہے تو اس اعتبار سے بھی ان دونوں میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔
دونوں مثالوں میں دوسرا فرق
و ههنا فرق آخر : ۔ یہاں سے ماتن انہی دو مثالوں میں اپنی طرف سے ایک فرق بیان فرما رہے ہیں۔ اور وہ یہ ہے کہ : ۔ لفظ اتی واحد منکر کو لاعلی التعیین شامل ہوتا ہے یعنی ایک فرد کو بغیر تعیین کے شامل ہوتا ہے تو اول صورت میں جب اس نے کہا ای عبیدی ضربک فھو تو حریت والا حکم غلاموں میں سے ایک غیر متعین غلام میں ثابت ہو گا لیکن اس غلام میں جس میں ضرب والا وصف پایا جائے اس بات سے قطع نظر کے اس کے غیر میں یہ وصف ہے یا نہیں ۔ تو اب جس غلام میں یہ وصف پایا جائے گا تو وہ اس وصف میں غیر سے قطع نظر منفرد ہوگا۔ اس لیے ان تمام غلاموں میں حریت والا حکم ثابت ہو جائے گا جن میں ضرب والا وصف پایا جائے گا کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک غیر سے قطع نظر کرتے ہوئے منفرد ہے تو وحدت والا معنی بھی باقی رہا اور حکم بھی عام ہو گیا۔ اگر یہاں عموم کو ثابت نہ کیا جائے یعنی اگر ہر ایک کا محقق ثابت نہ ہو تو پھر دو حال سے خالی نہیں یا تو عتق بعض کے لئے ثابت ہوگا اور بعض کے لئے ثابت نہیں ہوگا یا کسی کے لئے بھی حق ثابت نہیں ہوگا پہلی صورت تو اس لئے باطل ہے کہ اس میں ترجیح بلا مریخ لازم آتی ہے کیونکہ اولویت کسی غلام میں نہیں ہے اور دوسری صورت بھی باطل ہے کیونکہ جب محقق کا تمام افراد میں حکم ثابت نہیں ہوگا تو کلام باطل ہو جائے گی۔ اس لیے کلام کو باطل ہونے سے بچانے کے لیے عموم ماننا پڑے گا ۔ اور دوسری صورت میں ایسا نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس نے کہا ای عبیدی ضربت فھو متر اس نے ضرب کی نسبت مخاطب کی طرف کی ہے تو جس کو وہ پہلے مارے گاوہی آزاد ہو گا۔ اگر وہ سب کو اکٹھے مارتا ہے تو پھر بھی ایک ہی غلام میں اعتاق
The word 'ayy' is among them, and it is a nakirah (indefinite) that becomes general due to the attribute. So if he said, 'O my slave who struck you, he is free.' Then if all the slaves struck him, all of them become free. And if he said, 'O my slave whom you struck, he is free.' Then none is freed except one. The jurists gave this ruling because in the first case, the attribute of striking is mentioned and becomes general due to that attribute. And in the second case, the attribute is cut off from it. And this difference is problematic from a grammatical perspective because in the first case, the attribute is the striking (of the addressee) and in the second case, it is being struck (by the addressee). And here is another difference, and that is that 'ayy' includes only one indefinite individual. So in the first case (his saying 'O my slave who struck you, he is free'), the freedom (of a single indefinite slave) is suspended on his striking, without regard to others. So each one is freed on the basis that he is singular. So the singularity is not invalidated. And if this (freedom of each) is not established and some are not given precedence over others, then the speech would be invalidated entirely. And in the second case (his saying 'O my slave whom you struck, he is free'), one is established and the agent has a choice (since here it is possible to give the addressee a choice, unlike the first case). Like 'whatever hide I touch with tanning, it is purified' – this is similar to the first because its purification is suspended on its tanning without specifying an agent, so it indicates generality. And like 'any bread you want' – this is similar to the second because giving choice to the addressee is possible here. He is not able to eat every one, but he is able to eat every one, yet the addressee will have choice. And this type of speech is used for giving choice in custom. Ruling of the word 'ayy' among the words of generality. Explanation: Among them is 'ayy': it is one of the words of generality. Its original form is for a specific individual, but when it is described and its attribute is general, it becomes general. Its usage is with idafah (annexation). If it is annexed to a nakirah, its generality is obvious. If annexed to a ma'rifah (definite), then one undefined individual from among its individuals is intended in a distributive sense. For example, a man says 'O my slave who struck you, imagine' – here the attribute of 'ayy' is 'strike' which is general. Therefore 'ayy' is general. So if all slaves struck the addressee, all slaves become free. And if he said 'O my slave whom you struck, he is free' and the addressee struck all slaves, then not all slaves become free; only one slave becomes free, and the master has choice in selecting him. What is the difference between these two cases? The later uṣūlīs have stated this difference: in the first case, the attribute of 'ayy' is 'strike' which, when attributed to the slaves, is general, so 'ayy' is general. In the second case, this attribute is cut off from 'ayy' and attributed to the addressee, so it is not general. But the author (al-mātīn) refuted this difference and said: what is meant by attribute? Lexical or grammatical attribute? If lexical attribute is meant, then this attribute exists in both cases; the difference is only that in the first case the attribute is striking (by the addressee) and in the second it is being struck (by the addressee). So there is no difference in terms of attribute. And if grammatical attribute is meant, then it does not exist in either case, because according to all grammarians the word 'ayy' is either a conditional particle or a relative pronoun, and the clause after it is either its response or its clause (silal), not an attribute. So from this perspective also, there is no difference between them. The second difference in the two examples. Here the author presents from himself a difference in these two examples. It is that the word 'ayy' includes one indefinite individual without specification. So in the first case, when he said 'O my slave who struck you, he is free', the ruling of freedom is established in one unspecified slave from among the slaves, but in that slave in whom the attribute of striking is found, regardless of whether this attribute is found in others or not. So whichever slave is found to have this attribute, he is singular in this attribute without regard to others. Therefore, the ruling of freedom is established in all slaves who have the attribute of striking, because each one of them is singular when disregarding others. So the meaning of singularity remains and the ruling becomes general. If generality were not established here, i.e., if each individual's freedom were not proven, then two possibilities arise: either freedom is established for some and not for others, or it is not established for anyone. The first is invalid because it entails preference without a preponderant factor, since no slave has priority. The second is also invalid because if the ruling is not established for all the individuals indicated by the word, the speech would be invalid. Therefore, to avoid the invalidation of the speech, we must accept generality. And in the second case, it is not so, because he said 'O my slave whom you struck, he is free' and he attributed the striking to the addressee. So whichever one he strikes first, that one is freed. If he strikes them all together, still only one slave is freed.
Source pages p. 621

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the word 'ayy' (أي) as one of the words of generality (alfaz al-'umum) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It presents two disputed interpretations of a conditional expression involving manumission of slaves.

The author begins by stating that 'ayy' is an indefinite noun (nakirah) that becomes general ('amm) when it is followed by a general attribute (sifah). Two examples are given: (1) 'ayy 'abidi darabak fa-huwa hurr' (any slave of mine who struck you is free) and (2) 'ayy 'abidi darabtahu fa-huwa hurr' (any slave of mine whom you struck is free). The juristic ruling: if all slaves struck in the first case, all are freed; in the second, only one is freed.

The first difference (attributed to some usuli scholars) is based on the attribute: in the first, the attribute 'darabak' is general and attached to 'ayy'; in the second, it is cut off and attached to the addressee. The author (al-matin) refutes this, arguing that lexically both have a similar attribute (striking versus being struck), and grammatically the clause after 'ayy' is not an attribute but a completion of the conditional or relative clause. So this difference is invalid.

The author then proposes a second difference: 'ayy' includes only one indefinite individual without specification. In the first case, the freedom of each slave is conditional on his own act of striking, independent of others. Since each slave individually performs the striking, each is unique in that act relative to himself, so all slaves who strike must be freed. To hold otherwise would lead to either unjust preference or nullification of the speech. Hence, generality is necessary. In the second case, the act is attributed to the addressee (the master), who can only strike one slave at a time effectively. Thus, only the first he strikes (or one he chooses) is freed. The choice remains with the addressee.

The passage also draws analogies: the first case is like 'ayyuma asaba dabigh fa-qad tuhira' (any hide struck by tanning becomes pure), where the purification is tied to the act without specifying an agent, indicating generality. The second is like 'kullu ayyi khubzin turid' (any bread you want), where choice is given to the addressee.

This analysis is Hanafi in its approach, emphasizing linguistic analysis and logical necessity to preserve the coherence of speech.

Key Terms
أي (ayy): A word used for generality when annexed to a noun and followed by a general attribute; it includes one indefinite individual in a distributive sense.
عموم (umum): Generality; a word that covers all individuals of its category without exception.
صفة (sifah): Attribute; in this context, the clause that describes the individual intended by 'ayy'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi view, why does the expression 'ayy 'abidi darabak fa-huwa hurr' free all slaves when all strike the addressee?
A) Because the attribute 'darabak' is attached to 'ayy' and is general.
B) Because each slave individually performs the striking, so each is the intended indefinite individual of 'ayy'.
C) Because the word 'ayy' by its nature covers all slaves.
D) Because the addressee has no choice in the matter.

Key Takeaway
The word 'ayy' becomes general not due to the attribute but due to the distributive nature of the indefinite individual when the act is attributed to the subject themselves.
