Second Distinction: 'أي' as Indefinite Singular Leading to Generality
Explains a second distinction between two examples of conditional sentences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that 'أي' in conditional statements indicates generality when the condition is an attribute of the subject, and explain the logical reasoning (tarjih bila murajjih) that necessitates this interpretation.

Core Idea
When 'أي' ties a ruling to a subject's attribute, generality is logically necessary; when tied to the speaker's act, choice limits the ruling.

The Hanafi school's linguistic analysis often turns on subtle differences. This lesson dissects a second distinction between two conditional sentences: one where the condition is an attribute of the subject and another where it is an act of the speaker. By tracing the logical necessity of generality in the first, we see how the principle 'avoiding preference without a preferrer' forces a general interpretation. This is a classic case of istinbat based on linguistic structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
Example 1: 'أي عبيدي ضربك فھو حر' (any slave of mine who strikes you is free). Example 2: 'أي عبيدي ضربتك فھو حر' (any slave of mine I strike is free). Notice the verb form: one attributes striking to the slave, the other to the speaker.
Both use 'أي' as an indefinite singular.
2
Analyze the word 'أي' as indefinite singular
In Hanafi usul, 'أي' here is a singular indefinite (نكرة) that refers to one unspecified individual. It does not by itself indicate number; its scope depends on linguistic context.
3
Apply the logical argument in Example 1
If we do not apply the ruling to all striking slaves, we face two impossible outcomes: either we free some without reason (ترجيح بلا مرجح) or free none (making the speech futile). Thus, generality is necessary to save the speech from absurdity.
This is a rational (عقلي) argument.
4
Apply the reasoning to Example 2
Here the verb 'ضربتك' attributes striking to the speaker, so the speaker's choice (اختيار) acts as a preferrer. Thus, the ruling applies only to the one the speaker actually strikes, avoiding the logical problem.
Choice serves as a valid preferrer.
5
Connect to the analogies
The hide tanning hadith ('أيما إهاب دبغ فقد طھر') mirrors Example 1: no agent specified, so generality applies. The bread example ('كل آي تیز ترید') mirrors Example 2: the addressee's choice limits the ruling.
The analogies clarify the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ههنا فرق آخر : ۔ یہاں سے ماتن انہی دو مثالوں میں اپنی طرف سے ایک فرق بیان فرما رہے ہیں۔ اور وہ یہ ہے کہ : ۔ لفظ اتی واحد منکر کو لاعلی التعیین شامل ہوتا ہے یعنی ایک فرد کو بغیر تعیین کے شامل ہوتا ہے تو اول صورت میں جب اس نے کہا ای عبیدی ضربک فھو تو حریت والا حکم غلاموں میں سے ایک غیر متعین غلام میں ثابت ہو گا لیکن اس غلام میں جس میں ضرب والا وصف پایا جائے اس بات سے قطع نظر کے اس کے غیر میں یہ وصف ہے یا نہیں ۔ تو اب جس غلام میں یہ وصف پایا جائے گا تو وہ اس وصف میں غیر سے قطع نظر منفرد ہوگا۔ اس لیے ان تمام غلاموں میں حریت والا حکم ثابت ہو جائے گا جن میں ضرب والا وصف پایا جائے گا کیونکہ ان میں سے ہر ایک غیر سے قطع نظر کرتے ہوئے منفرد ہے تو وحدت والا معنی بھی باقی رہا اور حکم بھی عام ہو گیا۔ اگر یہاں عموم کو ثابت نہ کیا جائے یعنی اگر ہر ایک کا محقق ثابت نہ ہو تو پھر دو حال سے خالی نہیں یا تو عتق بعض کے لئے ثابت ہوگا اور بعض کے لئے ثابت نہیں ہوگا یا کسی کے لئے بھی حق ثابت نہیں ہوگا پہلی صورت تو اس لئے باطل ہے کہ اس میں ترجیح بلا مریخ لازم آتی ہے کیونکہ اولویت کسی غلام میں نہیں ہے اور دوسری صورت بھی باطل ہے کیونکہ جب محقق کا تمام افراد میں حکم ثابت نہیں ہوگا تو کلام باطل ہو جائے گی۔ اس لیے کلام کو باطل ہونے سے بچانے کے لیے عموم ماننا پڑے گا ۔ اور دوسری صورت میں ایسا نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس نے کہا ای عبیدی ضربت فھو متر اس نے ضرب کی نسبت مخاطب کی طرف کی ہے تو جس کو وہ پہلے مارے گاوہی آزاد ہو گا۔ اگر وہ سب کو اکٹھے مارتا ہے تو پھر بھی ایک ہی غلام میں اعتاق ثابت ہوگا تمام میں نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اس میں ترجیح بلا مرج بھی نہیں ہے کیونکہ تمام کو مارنے کی صورت میں اختیار فاعل مخاطب کو ہوگا جس کو وہ اختیار کرے گاوہ آزاد ہو جائے گا اور یہی اختیار مرجح ہو گا اس لیے ثانی صورت میں ایک غلام آزاد ہو گا نہ کہ تمام افراد۔
اس کے بعد ماتن نے دونوں مثالوں کو ایک ایک نظیر کے ذریعہ واضح کیا ہے اول کی نظیر ہے۔ نبی اکرم کا یہ فرمان " ایما اهاب دبغ فقد طهر جس چھڑے کو دباغت دے دی جائے وہ پاک ہے۔ یہاں چھڑے کی طہارت کو معلق کیا گیا ہے اس کی دباغت کیساتھ ۔ اور اس میں یہ ذکر نہیں ہے کہ دباغت دینے والا کون ہو؟ یعنی کسی فائل کی تعین نہیں کی گئی۔ اس لیے جس چمڑے میں دباغت والا وصف پایا جائے گا وہ طاہر ہو جائے گا اور یہاں بھی اول صورت میں اسی طرح ہے کہ غلام کے اعتماق کو ضار بیت والے وصف کے پائے جانے کے ساتھ معلق کیا گیا ہے تو جس غلام میں یہ وصف پایا جائے گا وہ آزاد ہو جائے گا۔ اور دوسری صورت کی نظیر یہ ہے کہ کوئی آدمی دوسرے کو کہے کل آئی تیز ترید یہاں مخاطب کی طرف ارادے کی نسبت کی گئی ہے اس لیے یہاں فاعل مخاطب کی جانب سے تغیر ممکن ہے اس لیے جس کو اختیار کرے گا صرف اسی روٹی کو کھانا اسکے لیے صحیح ہوگا تمام روٹیوں کو کھانا درست نہیں ہوگا بالکل اسی طرح مذکورہ دوسری صورت میں فاعل مخاطب کی جانب سے تخیر ممکن ہے اس لیے وہاں بھی وہی غلام آزاد ہو گا جس کو وہ اختیار کرے گا تمام غلام آزاد نہیں ہوں گے۔
And here is another distinction: From here, the matn author presents a distinction of his own in these two examples. And it is that: The word 'ay' includes an indefinite singular without specification, i.e., it includes one individual without determination. So in the first case, when he said 'any of my slaves who strikes you, he is free', the ruling of freedom is established for one unspecified slave from among the slaves, but only in that slave in whom the attribute of striking is found, regardless of whether this attribute exists in others or not. So now, whichever slave is found to have this attribute, he is unique in this attribute irrespective of others. Therefore, the ruling of freedom becomes established in all those slaves in whom the attribute of striking is found, because each one of them is unique when disregarding others, so the meaning of singularity remains and the ruling becomes general. If generality were not established here, i.e., if each individual's [share of the ruling] is not proven, then two cases are possible: either freedom is proven for some and not for others, or it is not proven for anyone at all. The first case is invalid because it entails preference without a preferrer, since there is no preference among the slaves. The second case is also invalid because if the ruling is not proven for all individuals, the speech becomes futile. Therefore, to avoid futility of speech, we must accept generality. And in the second case, it is not so because he said 'any of my slaves whom I strike, he is free' – he attributed the striking to the addressee, so whichever one he strikes first, that one will be free. If he strikes all together, still emancipation is established for one slave only, not all. And there is no preference without a preferrer here, because in the case of striking all, the choice belongs to the acting addressee; whichever he chooses will be free, and this choice is the preferrer. Hence, in the second case, one slave is freed, not all individuals. Afterwards, the matn author clarifies the two examples with analogies. The analogy for the first is the Prophet's statement, 'Any hide that is tanned becomes pure.' Here, the purity of the hide is suspended on its tanning, and it is not mentioned who does the tanning – no agent is specified. So whichever hide is found to have the attribute of tanning becomes pure. Here also, in the first case, the emancipation of the slave is suspended on the existence of the striking attribute, so whichever slave has this attribute becomes free. The analogy for the second case is: a man says to another, 'Eat any bread that you desire.' Here, desire is attributed to the addressee, so the acting addressee can exercise choice, so only the bread he chooses may be eaten by him; it is not permissible to eat all the loaves. Exactly like that, in the mentioned second case, the addressee can exercise choice, so that slave becomes free whom he chooses; not all slaves become free.
Source pages p. 622

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis where the commentator (matn) provides a second distinction between two examples of conditional sentences used in usul al-fiqh. The first example is 'أي عبيدي ضربك فحر' (any of my slaves who strikes you is free), and the second is 'أي عبيدي ضربتك فحر' (any of my slaves I strike is free). The distinction hinges on the word 'أي' (any) which, in its basic usage, indicates an indefinite singular. In the first example, the predicate 'strikes you' is attributed to the subject (the slave), so the attribute of striking is distributed among the slaves who perform the act. Since each slave who strikes is individually unique in possessing that attribute at the moment of speaking, the ruling of freedom applies to all such slaves. The commentator argues that if we did not accept generality here, we would face either arbitrary preference or nullification of the speech, so the only valid interpretation is generality. In the second example, 'striking' is attributed to the speaker (the master), so the master's act of striking is deliberate; he can choose whom to strike, and thus only the one he actually strikes (or chooses) is freed. The commentator supports this with analogies: the hadith about tanning hides (where the act of tanning is not attributed to any person, so any tanned hide becomes pure) and the bread example (where the addressee chooses which bread to eat). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of linguistic analysis in determining the scope of rulings.

Key Terms
أي (ay): An indefinite singular word meaning 'any', used to refer to an unspecified individual.
عموم (umum): Generality; the inclusion of all individuals that satisfy the condition.
وحدت (wahdat): Singularity; the quality of being one, which in this context applies to each individual slave when considered separately.
ترجیح بلا مرجح (tarjih bila murajjih): Preference without a preferrer; an invalid logical consequence that would arise if the ruling were applied to some but not all equally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example (أي عبيدي ضربك فحر), why does the ruling of freedom apply to all slaves who strike?
A) Because the word 'أي' indicates generality unconditionally.
B) Because each slave who strikes is individually unique in having the attribute, and not accepting generality leads to logical inconsistency.
C) Because the master intended to free all striking slaves.
D) Because the striking is attributed to the master, making all slaves equally subject.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method requires generality when a ruling is attached to a subject's attribute to avoid logical inconsistency; when tied to the speaker's action, choice saves the speech.

The Particle 'مَنْ': Generality versus Partiality in Conditional Clauses
Parsing the particle 'مَنْ' in usul al-fiqh: particular vs general usage and an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two usages of 'مَنْ' (general and particular) and apply the Hanafi rule that determines the ruling when 'مَنْ' enters upon a noun with parts, comparing the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
'مَنْ' can indicate generality or partiality; when followed by 'مِنْ' with a noun that has parts, Abu Hanifa interprets it as partial, while the Sahibayn treat it as general.

Particles like 'مَنْ' (who/whosoever) are not always straightforward in Arabic; their interpretation can shift a ruling from general to specific. This lesson unpacks an intra-Hanafi dispute where a single particle leads to two different fiqh outcomes. You will learn to parse the linguistic clues that determine whether 'مَنْ' is general (amm) or particular (khass) and understand why the Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa differed on a master's declaration of emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Classify the two uses of 'مَنْ'
The passage states that 'مَنْ' occurs as particular (e.g., Quranic verse about hypocrites) and as general when used as a conditional particle (e.g., 'Whoever enters...'). Identify these two uses and note the linguistic context for each.
The Quranic example shows 'مَنْ' referring to a specific group; the conditional example shows universal application.
2
Parse the disputed statement
Break down the master's statement: 'مَنْ شَاءَ مِنْ عَبِيدِي عِتْقَهُ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ'. Identify the conditional 'مَنْ', the preposition 'مِنْ', and the noun 'عَبِيدِي'. Consider how 'مِنْ' affects the scope of 'مَنْ'.
The presence of 'مِنْ' after 'مَنْ' is key to the dispute.
3
Identify the two interpretations
State the position of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): 'مَنْ' is general, and 'مِنْ عَبِيدِي' is explanatory (bayān), so if all slaves wish, all are freed. Then state Abu Hanifa's view: 'مَنْ' indicates partiality (tab‘īḍ) because it enters upon a noun with parts, so only all but one are freed.
The Sahibayn act on 'the word of generality (عموم)', while Abu Hanifa relies on the principle of tab‘īḍ.
4
Apply the usul principles
For Abu Hanifa, the underlying principle is 'Idha dakhala ‘ala dhi ab‘adin fahuwa li al-tab‘īḍ' (when it enters upon something with parts, it is for partiality). For the Sahibayn, the principle is that the conditional 'مَنْ' retains its generality, and 'مِنْ' clarifies (bayān) rather than particularizes.
Both principles are valid within Hanafi usul; the dispute is over which principle applies here.
5
Derive the practical ruling (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: if all slaves wish emancipation, Abu Hanifa's ruling frees all but one; the Sahibayn's ruling frees all. This shows how a linguistic analysis directly affects fiqh outcomes.
The passage does not specify which ruling is mufta bihi; both are recorded as valid positions.

وَمِنْهَا مَنْ وَهُوَ يَقَعُ خَاصًا كَقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَسْتَمِعُونَ إِلَيْكَ وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ يَنظُرُ إِلَيْكَ فَإِنَّ الْمُرَادَ بَعْضُ مَخْصُوصِ مِنْ الْمُنَافِقِينَ وَيَقَعُ عَامَّا فِي الْعُقَلاءِ إِذَا كَانَ لِلشَّرْطِ نَحْوُ مَنْ دَخَلَ دَارَ أَبِي سُفْيَانَ رضى الله عنه فَهُوَ آمِنٌ فَإِنْ قَالَ مَنْ شَاءَ مِنْ عَبِيدِى عِتْقَهُ فَهُوَ حُرِّ فَشَاء وَا عَتَقُوا وَفِيمَنْ شِئْتَ مِنْ عَبِيدِي عِتْقَهُ فَاعْتِقَهُ فَشَاءَ الْكُلَّ يُعْتَقَ الكُلُّ عِنْدَهُمَا عَمَلًا بِكَلِمَةِ الْعُمُومِ وَمِنْ لِلْبَيَانِ وَعِندَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ رحمه الله يَعْتِقُهُمْ إِلَّا وَاحِدًا لَّانَّ مِنْ لِلتَّبْعِيضِ إِذَا دَخَلَ عَلَى ذِي أَبْعَاضٍ كَمَا فِي كُلِّ مِنْ هَذَا الْخُبْزِ وَلَأَنَّهُ مُتَيَقَّنْ) أَى الْبَعْضُ مُتَيَقَّنٌ لأنَّ مِنْ إِذَا كَانَ لِلتَّبْعِيضِ فَظَاهِرٌ وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلْبَيَانِ فَالْبَعْضُ مُرَادٌ فَإِرَادَةُ الْبَعْضِ
And among them is 'man', which occurs as particular like His saying, 'And among them are some who listen to you, and among them are some who look at you' – for the intended meaning is a specific group of the hypocrites. And it occurs as general for rational beings when it is for the condition, like 'Whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) is safe'. So if he says 'Whoever among my slaves wishes his emancipation, he is free' – they wished and they were emancipated? [unclear] – And 'In whomever you wish among my slaves [his] emancipation, set him free' – if he wishes all, all are freed according to them (the two, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), acting upon the word of generality, and 'man' is for explanation (bayān). And according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), he frees all except one, because 'man' is for partiality (tab‘īḍ) when it enters upon something that has parts, as in 'each of this bread', and because that part is certain – i.e., the part is certain, because if 'man' is for partiality, it is apparent, and if it is for explanation, the part is intended, so intending the part...
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute regarding the particle 'مَنْ' (man). It begins by noting that 'مَنْ' can be used for particularity (like in the Quranic verses referring to some hypocrites) and for generality when used as a conditional particle (e.g., 'Whoever enters...'). The key dispute arises in a scenario: a master says 'مَنْ شَاءَ مِنْ عَبِيدِي عِتْقَهُ فَهُوَ حُرٌّ' (whichever of my slaves wishes his emancipation, he is free). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), if all the slaves wish, all become free, because they treat 'مَنْ' as a conditional particle indicating generality, and the phrase 'مِنْ عَبِيدِي' is considered explanatory (bayān). However, Abu Hanifa rules that only all but one are freed, because he interprets 'مَنْ' in this construction as partial (tab‘īḍ) when it enters upon a noun that has parts (like 'slaves'). He analogizes to the phrase 'كُلِّ مِنْ هَذَا الْخُبْزِ' (each from this bread), which indicates a part. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that 'مَنْ' for partiality explicitly indicates a part, and even if it were for explanation, a part is still intended. Thus, Abu Hanifa's ruling avoids freeing all, preserving the meaning of partiality. This illustrates how the interpretation of a single particle can lead to different rulings between the Hanafi imams themselves (Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn) and highlights the importance of linguistic analysis in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
مَنْ (man): A particle that can be either conditional (general) or partial (particular) depending on context.
تبعيض (tab‘īḍ): Partiality; indicating a part of a whole, as in 'مِنْ' for partiality.
بيان (bayān): Explanation; here, 'مِنْ' is considered explanatory, clarifying the previous noun.
عموم (umum): Generality; the inclusion of all individuals in the ruling.
شرط (shart): Condition; 'مَنْ' as a conditional particle introduces a condition.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'مَنْ' as general or particular depends on its context and whether it enters upon a noun with parts, demonstrating that usul analysis directly shapes fiqh rulings.

The Word 'مَنْ' and the Hanafi Dispute on Generality vs Partition
Usul dispute on the scope of 'مَنْ' in conditional clauses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the word 'مَنْ' and apply the Hanafi rule of tabʿīḍ when 'مَنْ' enters upon 'dhī abʿāḍ'.

Core Idea
When 'مَنْ' enters upon something with parts, it indicates partition — only some are intended, not all.

This passage examines a classic usul dispute about the scope of the word 'مَنْ'. While 'مَنْ' can be general in conditional sentences, its meaning shifts when the object has parts. Abu Hanifa maintains that it then implies partiality, whereas the Sahibayn treat it as always general. Understanding this nuance is essential for deriving rulings in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dual nature of 'مَنْ'
Note that 'مَنْ' can be particular (khāṣṣ) when referring to specific individuals, as in the Quranic verses about hypocrites, and general (ʿāmm) when used in conditional clauses for rational beings, e.g., 'Whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe.'
The context determines its usage.
2
Analyze the dispute scenarios
Two scenarios are given: (1) 'Whichever of my slaves wills his emancipation, he is free' — if all will, they are all freed by both sides, but the reasoning differs. (2) 'Whomever you will from my slaves, emancipate him' — if the master wills all, the Sahibayn free all, but Abu Hanifa frees all except one.
The key difference lies in whether the will is individual or collective.
3
Compare the proofs
The Sahibayn rely on the generality of 'مَنْ' and interpret 'مِنْ' as bayān (clarification). Abu Hanifa relies on the partitive meaning (tabʿīḍ) when 'مَنْ' enters upon 'dhī abʿāḍ', citing the analogy 'كُلْ مِنْ هَذَا الْخُبْزِ' (eat from this bread) where 'مِنْ' means some, not all.
Abu Hanifa also argues that the part is certain while the whole is probable.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the second scenario, the Sahibayn free all slaves, Abu Hanifa frees all except one. This shows how the usul principle directly impacts the fiqh ruling.
The first scenario yields the same outcome but with different reasoning.
5
Reflect on the commentator's unique distinction
The commentator notes that the distinction between the two scenarios — preserving partition in the first but negating it in the second — is uniquely his contribution.
This highlights the dynamic nature of usul analysis.

And among them is 'مَنْ', and it occurs as particular like His saying 'And among them are those who listen to you and among them are those who look at you' for the intended meaning is some specific hypocrites. And it occurs as general in the case of rational beings when it is for a condition, like 'Whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan (may Allah be pleased with him) is safe.' So if he said 'Whichever of my slaves wills his emancipation, he is free' and they all will, they are all freed. And in 'Whomever you will from my slaves, emancipate him' and he wills all, they are all freed according to them (the two companions) acting upon the word of generality and 'مَنْ' is for clarification (bayān). And according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) they are all freed except one, because 'مَنْ' is for partition (tab'īḍ) when it enters upon something with parts, as in 'eat from this bread' and because it is certain – meaning the part is certain because when 'مَنْ' is for partition it is apparent, and if it is for clarification then the part is intended, so intending the part is certain and intending the whole is probable, so it is necessary to observe both generality and partition. And in the first issue this is observed because the emancipation of each is suspended on his will regardless of others, so each one in this consideration is a part – i.e., each one regardless of others is a part of the whole, so each one is freed with the observance of partition. Unlike 'whomever you will' because if the addressed wills all, then the will of all is combined in him, so partition becomes void. And this distinction and the last distinction are among the things in which I am unique.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the word 'مَنْ' (man). It begins by noting that 'مَنْ' can be used for particular (khāṣṣ), as in the Qur'anic verses about hypocrites, where it refers to a specific subset. It can also be used as a general word (ʿāmm) when it appears in conditional clauses, e.g., 'Whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe.' The main dispute arises in a scenario where a master says 'Whichever of my slaves wills his emancipation, he is free' – if all slaves will it, they are all freed according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sāḥibayn) because they treat 'مَنْ' as a word of generality (ʿumūm) and interpret 'مِنْ' as bayān (clarification). They argue that 'مَنْ' is general for rational beings, so all are included. However, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that all are freed except one. His reasoning: 'مَنْ' indicates tabʿīḍ (partition) when it enters upon something that has parts ('dhī abʿāḍ'), as in the phrase 'كُلْ مِنْ هَذَا الْخُبْزِ' (eat from this bread) – meaning a part, not all of it. Since slaves are individuals with parts (each slave is a part of the group), 'مَنْ' here implies some, not all. He also argues that the intention of a part is certain (mutaʾayyan) while intention of the whole is merely probable (muḥtamal), so one must observe both generality and partition. He then explains that in the first example ('Whichever of my slaves wills...') each slave's freedom depends on his own will, so each one is considered a part, and thus all are freed while still honoring partition (since each act of freeing is separate). In contrast, in the second example ('Whomever you will from my slaves...') the addressing is to the master, and if he wills all, that will is a single comprehensive will negating partition, so all become free except one according to Abu Hanifa. The commentator (maybe the author of al-Tarwīḥ) notes that this distinction is unique to him.

Key Terms
مَنْ (man): A word that can be used for particular (khāṣṣ) when referring to specific individuals, and for general (ʿāmm) when used in conditional clauses for rational beings. In Hanafi usul, its meaning varies: it can indicate generality (ʿumūm) or partition (tabʿīḍ) depending on context.
التَّبْعِيض (al-tabʿīḍ): Partition or partiality: indicating that only a part of a whole is intended, not the entire set. Here, when 'مِنْ' is used for tabʿīḍ, it means 'some of'.
الْبَيَان (al-bayān): Clarification: when 'مِنْ' is used to clarify the kind or type, not to indicate partiality. Here, the Sāḥibayn interpret 'مِنْ' as bayān in 'مِنْ عَبِيدِي', meaning 'from among my slaves' without implying a part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if a master says 'Whomever you will from my slaves, emancipate him' and the master wills all?
A) All slaves are freed.
B) All slaves are freed except one.
C) Only one slave is freed.
D) No slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The word 'مَنْ' can be both general and partitive; when it enters upon 'dhī abʿāḍ', partition is presumed, and the outcome depends on whether the will is individual or collective.

The Word 'Man' (من) between Generality and Partiality: The Hanafi Dispute
Analyzing 'man' for generality and partiality with Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute over the word 'man' in conditional sentences and apply the principle of tab'īḍ when 'man' enters upon something with parts (dhī abʿāḍ).

Core Idea
When 'man' enters upon something with parts, Hanafi usul reads it as partiality (tab'īḍ), not generality — certainty outweighs probability.

This lesson examines a classic usul al-fiqh dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, regarding the interpretation of the word 'man' (whoever) in the phrase "man shi'ta min 'abīdī ʿtiqhu fahuwa ḥurr" (whoever you will of my slaves, free him). The disagreement hinges on whether 'man' retains its default generality or becomes a word of partiality when it governs a noun that refers to a collective with parts. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze linguistic indicators in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested phrase and the two interpretations
Pinpoint the exact phrase under dispute: 'man shi'ta min 'abīdī ʿtiqhu fahuwa ḥurr'. Note that Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'man' as indicating partiality (tab'īḍ), while the Sahibayn interpret it as general (ʿumūm) with the second 'man' being explanatory (bayāniyyah).
The first phrase 'man shā'a min 'abīdī' is agreed to be general.
2
Follow the Sahibayn's argument
The Sahibayn argue that 'man' is inherently a word of generality, and the 'man' in 'min 'abīdī' explains the category without restricting generality. Therefore, if the master wills all, all are freed.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's first proof: tab'īḍ upon dhī abʿāḍ
Abu Hanifa notes that 'min 'abīdī' refers to something with parts (multiple slaves). According to linguistic usage, when 'man' enters upon such a noun, it indicates partiality. Analogous to 'kullu min hādhā al-khubz' (each from this bread), where one cannot take the whole loaf.
This is a qiyas from everyday speech.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's second proof: certainty over probability
Whether 'man' is taken as tab'īḍ or bayāniyyah, the partial meaning (some) is certain: if tab'īḍ, it explicitly means some; if bayāniyyah, the some are still included when intending all. Taking all is merely probable. The certain must be preferred.
This is a rational argument rooted in usul preference.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
If the master wills all slaves free: according to the Sahibayn, all are freed; according to Abu Hanifa, all except one are freed, with the master choosing which if simultaneous, or the last if sequential.

مُتَيَقَّنَةٌ وَإِرَادَةُ الْكُلِّ مُحْتَمَلَةٌ فَوَجَبَ رِعَايَةُ الْعُمُومِ وَالتَّبْعِيضِ وَفِي الْمَسْأَلَةِ الْأُولَى هَذَا مُرَاعَى لِانْ عِتَقَ كُلِّ مُعَلَّقٍ بِمَشِيئَتِهِ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ غَيْرِهِ فَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ بِهَذَا الاعْتِبَارِ بَعْضُ أَى كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ غَيْرِهِ بَعْضٌ مِنْ الْمَجْمُوع فَيُعْتَقُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مَعَ رِعَايَةِ التَّبْعِيضِ بِخِلَافِ مَنْ شِئْت فَإِنَّ الْمُخَاطَبَ إنْ شَاءَ الْكُلَّ فَمَشِيئَةُ الْكُلِّ مُجْتَمِعَةٌ فِيهِ فَيَبْطُلُ التَّبْعِيضُ وَهَذَا الْفَرْقُ وَالْفَرْقُ الْأَخِيرُ فِي أَيِّ مِمَّا تَفَرَّدْتُ بِهِ . ترجمہ: ۔ اور ان ( الفاظ عام ) میں سے ایک من ہے اور واقع ہوتا ہے خاص جیسے اللہ تعالیٰ کا قول ومنهم من يستمعون الیک ولهم من نظر الیک ہے کیونکہ مراد بعض مخصوص ہیں منافقین میں سے ( اور واقع ہوتا ہے عام ذوی المعقول میں جبکہ ہو وہ شرط کیلئے جیسے من دخل دار ابی سفیان (رضی اللہ عنہ فھو آمین۔ پس اگر اس نے کہا کہ من شاء من عبیدی منتقہ فھوئے ۔ پس ان سب نے چاہا تو وہ سب آزاد ہو جائیں گے اور من شئت من عبيدى عنقه فاعتقه " میں پس اس نے چاہا کل کو تو آزاد ہو جائیں گے تمام صاحبین کے ہاں عمل کرتے ہوئے کلمہ عموم پر اور من بیان کے لیے ہوگا۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے ہاں آزاد ہو جائیں گے وہ سارے سوائے ایک کے کیونکہ من تبعیض کے لیے آتا ہے جب کہ داخل ہو وہ ذوا بعاض پر جیسا كه كل من هذا الحجز میں (اور اس لیے کی وہ متیقن ہیں ) یعنی بعض یقینی ہیں کیونکہ من جب تبعیض کے لیے ہو تو ظاہر ہے اور اگر بیان کے لئے ہو تو بھی اگر کل مراد لیں گے تو بعض پھر بھی مراد ہوں گے تو بعض کو مراد لینا یقینی ہو گا اور کل کا اراد محتمل ہوگا ( پس واجب ہوگا رعایت کرنا عموم کی اور تبعیض کی اور مسئلہ اولی میں اس کی رعایت کی گئی ہے کیونکہ ہر ایک کی آزادی معلق ہے اس کی مشیت کیسا تھ قطع نظر کرنے اس کے غیر کے تو ہر ایک اس اعتبار سے بعض ہے ) یعنی ہر ایک قطع نظر اس کے غیر سے بعض ہے مجموعہ کا پس آزاد ہو جائے گا ہر ایک تبعیض کی رعایت کرنے کیساتھ بخلاف من شمت کے کیونکہ مخاطب اگر چاہے گا کل کو تو کل کی مشیت مجمع ہے اس میں پس باطل ہو جائے گی تبعیض اور یہ فرق اور دوسرا فرق ہستی میں ان اشیاء میں سے ہیں جن کو ذکر کرنے میں میں منفرد ہوں۔
دفع عام واقع ہوتا ہے۔ تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ اس کا استعمال چار طریقہ پر ہوتا ہے (۱) کبھی یہ شرط کے معنی کو مضمون ہوتا ہے جیسے من جائی فلہ دور ہم (۲) کبھی یہ استفہام کے معنی میں مستعمل ہوتا ہے جیسے من فی الدار (۳) کبھی من موصول کے معنی میں ہوتا ہے (۴) کبھی من موصوفہ ہوتا ہے جیسے مصنف نے اس کی مثال بیان کی ہے تو پہلی دوصورتوں میں من عام ہوتا ہے اور آخری دو صورتوں میں خاص ہوتا ہے۔ خاص کی مثال جیسے ومنهم من يستمعون اليك ومنهم من ينظر اليک یہاں " من " سے منافقین کی طرف اشارہ ہے لیکن اس سے تمام منافقین مراد نہیں بعض مراد ہیں اور " من عام اس وقت ہوتا ہے جب وہ شرط یا استفہامیہ واقع ہورہا ہو جیسے من دخل دار ابی سفیان فهو آمن یہاں من عام ہے کہ جو بھی ابوسفیان کے گھر میں داخل ہوا اس کو امان ہے کسی کی تخصیص نہیں ہے۔ فاذ قال من شاء من عبیدی علقہ فھو تر " یہاں سے بیان فرما رہے ہیں کہ گر کسی آدمی نے کہا کہ من شاء من عبیدی عتقہ فھو خر تو یہاں من عام ہو گا اور اس کے غلاموں میں سے جو جو اپنی آزادی چاہے گا آزاد ہو جائے گا حتی کہ اگر تمام غلام اپنی آزادی کے خواہش مند ہوئے تو تمام آزاد ہو جائیں گے لیکن اگر یہ کہا کہ من شئت من عبیدی عقہ فھو تر تو صاحبین کے نزدیک تو من عام ہوگا کہ اگر مخاطب تمام کی آزادی کو چاہتا ہے تو تمام آزاد ہو جائیں گے۔ دلیل صاحبین : یہ دلیل دیتے ہیں کہ یہاں من کلمہ عموم میں سے ہے اس کا تقاضہ یہی ہے کہ اس کو عام مانا جائے اس لیے یہ عام ہوگا باقی جو من عبیدی میں من ہے یہ بیانیہ ہو گا اور یہ اس عموم کا بیان ہے۔ وعند ابی حنیفہ ۔۔۔۔۔ لیکن امام صاحب کے نزدیک یہ من عام نہیں ہوگا اس لیے تمام کو چاہنے کی صورت میں بھی تمام آزاد نہیں ہوں گے بلکہ صرف ایک آزاد نہ ہوگا باقی سب آزاد ہو جا ئینگے اب اگر مخاطب نے ترتیب کے ساتھ آزاد کیا تو آخری آزاد نہیں ہو گا اور اگر اکٹھے سب کی آزادی کی نیت کی تو ایک آزاد نہ ہوگا اور اس کو متعین کرنے میں مخاطب کو اختیار ہوگا۔ امام اعظم رحمہ اللہ کی دلیل اول:۔ امام صاحب یہ وجہ بیان فرماتے ہے کہ یہاں من تبعیض کے لیے ہے اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب من ذی ابعاض (اجزاء والی ) شی پر داخل ہو تو اس وقت یہ تبعیض کے لئے ہوتا ہے یعنی اپنے مدخول کے بعض افراد پر دلالت کرتا ہے جیسے کل من هذا الخبز یہاں خبر ذی ابعاض ہے اور یہاں من تبعیضیہ ہے اسی لیے تو مخاطب کے لیے پوری روٹی کھانا درست نہیں ہے اسی طرح یہاں من عبیدی پر داخل ہے جو ذی ابعاض ہے اس لیے یہاں بھی تبعیضہ ہو گا۔ لھذا تمام غلام آزاد نہیں ہونگے۔ دلیل ثانی: ولانہ متقین:۔ یہاں سے دوسری دلیل دی ہے امام صاحب کی کہ یہاں بعض کو مراد لینا متقین ہے چاہے من بیا نیہ ہو چاہے تبعیضہ ہو۔ تبعیضہ کی صورت میں تو ظاہر ہے۔ بیانیہ کی صورت میں بھی اس لیے کہ جب تمام افراد مراد لیے
Certain and the intention of all is probable, so observing generality and partiality is obligatory. In the first case, this is observed because each is conditional on his will irrespective of others, so each is part of the whole. Thus each is freed with observance of partiality, unlike 'man shi'ta' because if the addressee wills all, the will of all is combined in him, so partiality is invalid. This distinction and the last distinction are among what I alone have mentioned. Translation: Among the words of generality is 'man', and it occurs as specific, like Allah's saying 'and among them are those who listen to you' and 'among them are those who look at you', because specific hypocrites are meant. It occurs as general in conditional contexts like 'whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe.' So if he said: 'Whoever of my slaves wills his freedom is free' then if all will it, all are freed. But if he said: 'Whoever you will of my slaves is free' then according to the Sahibayn, 'man' is general: if he wills all, all are freed. Their proof: 'man' is a word of generality; the 'man' in 'min 'abidi' is explanatory. According to Abu Hanifa, not all are freed; only all except one because 'man' is for partiality when it enters upon something with parts, like 'kullu min hadha al-khubz'. And because certainty: if it is for partiality it is clear, if explanatory then taking some is certain while taking all is probable. So observing generality and partiality is obligatory. In the first case this is observed because each's freedom is conditional on his will independent of others, so each is part of the whole. Thus each is freed with observance of partiality, unlike 'man shi'ta' because the will of all is combined. This distinction and the other are among what I alone have mentioned. It also occurs generally. Its usage is fourfold: (1) conditional, (2) interrogative, (3) relative, (4) descriptive. The first two are general, the last two specific. Example of specific: 'and among them are those who listen to you' – here not all hypocrites are meant. Example of general: 'whoever enters the house of Abu Sufyan is safe.' So if someone says: 'Whoever of my slaves wills is free' – here 'man' is general, all who will are freed. If he says: 'Whoever you will of my slaves is free' – according to the Sahibayn, it is general such that if he wills all, all are freed. Their proof: 'man' is a word of generality; the 'man' in 'min 'abidi' is explanatory. According to Abu Hanifa, it is not general; not all are freed; only all except one. If he frees in order, the last is not freed; if simultaneously, he chooses which remains. First proof of Abu Hanifa: 'man' here is for partiality because when it enters upon something with parts, it indicates some individuals, like 'kullu min hadha al-khubz'. Second proof: because it is certain: taking partiality is certain, whereas taking all is probable; the certain must be preferred.
Source pages p. 624

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the usul discussion regarding the word 'man' (من) as one of the words of generality. The author first presents the Arabic text from the matn (possibly Al-Tawdih), which argues that when 'man' is used in a conditional phrase like 'man shā'a min 'abīdī ʿtaqahu fahuwa ḥurr' (whoever of my slaves wills his freedom, he is free), the word 'man' retains its generality: all slaves who will freedom are freed, because each individual's will is considered independently, making each slave a 'part' of the whole, and thus partiality is observed. This is contrasted with the phrase 'man shi'ta min 'abīdī ʿtiqhu fahuwa ḥurr' (whoever you will of my slaves, free him), where the declaration is about the master's will; if the master wills all, then all are freed, because the will of all is combined in him, nullifying partiality. The author claims that this distinction and another are his own original contributions. Then the Urdu translation and commentary explain that 'man' is one of the words of generality but is not fixed; it sometimes indicates specific meaning, as in Quranic verses referring to specific hypocrites. It becomes general when used as a conditional or interrogative particle. The passage then focuses on two specific examples: (1) 'man shā'a min 'abīdī' – all scholars agree that 'man' here is general; thus if all slaves desire freedom, all are freed. (2) 'man shi'ta min 'abīdī' – there is ikhtilaf: the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it general, arguing that 'man' is a word of generality and the second 'man' in 'min 'abīdī' is explanatory (bayāniyyah). Therefore, if the master wills all, all are freed. Imam Abu Hanifa disagrees: according to him, 'man' here is for partiality (tab'īḍ), so not all are freed; instead, all except one are freed. The master has the choice of which one remains enslaved if he frees them simultaneously; if sequentially, the last is not freed. Abu Hanifa provides two proofs: first, when 'man' enters upon something that has parts (dhī abʿāḍ), it indicates partiality, like 'kullu min hādhā al-khubz' (each from this bread) – you cannot eat the whole loaf. Similarly, 'min 'abīdī' has parts (multiple slaves), so 'man' indicates only some. Second, taking a part (some) is certain (mutaqayyanah) in both interpretations: if 'man' is for tab'īḍ, it expressly means some; if it is bayāniyyah, even if one intends all, the some are still included, so taking some is certain whereas taking all is merely probable. Since certainty overrides probability, partiality must be observed. This passage exemplifies the Hanafi usul approach of linguistic analysis and preference for the certain over the probable in interpreting general words.

Key Terms
من (man): A word of generality that can be specific depending on context; when used as a conditional or interrogative it is general, when as a relative or descriptive it is specific.
تبعيض (tab'īḍ): Indicating partiality or some individuals from a group, as opposed to generality ('umūm).
بيان (bayān): Explanatory; when 'man' is bayāniyyah, it explains or specifies the preceding noun without restricting its generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the phrase 'man shi'ta min 'abīdī ʿtiqhu fahuwa ḥurr', what is the practical result according to Imam Abu Hanifa if the master wills all his slaves to be free?
A) All slaves are freed because 'man' is general.
B) None of the slaves are freed because the phrase is invalid.
C) All slaves except one are freed, and the master may choose which one remains enslaved.
D) Only one slave is freed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi interpretation of 'man' as tab'īḍ when governing dhī abʿāḍ reflects the usul principle of preferring certainty over probability.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate

The Generality of 'Ma' and the Interpretation of 'Min' in Hanafi Usul
Explaining 'ma' as a general particle and its metaphorical use, with an intra-Hanafi dispute on 'min'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that 'ma' is a general particle for non-intelligent beings and evaluate how the interpretation of 'min' (partitive vs. explanatory) affects the legal ruling on the number of divorces.

Core Idea
'Ma' is general; 'min' can be partitive or explanatory – the ruling on divorce numbers hinges on which meaning is preserved.

This lesson examines the usul al-fiqh concept of general words (alfaz al-umum) through the particle 'ma'. The text presents two illustrative cases: one where 'ma' governs a condition for freeing a slave-girl, and another where a dispute arises between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) over the interpretation of 'min' in a divorce delegation. Understanding the default meanings and possible metaphorical uses of these particles is essential for accurate legal derivation (istinbat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general particle
The text introduces 'ma' as a general particle (amm) primarily used for non-intelligent beings. It can be borrowed metaphorically for intelligent beings, but the default is generality.
The Arabic word 'ma' is a tool of generality in Hanafi usul.
2
Apply generality in the slave-girl case
In the example 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free', 'ma' covers everything in the womb. Since she gives birth to both a male and a female, the condition of totality fails, so she is not freed.
The ruling depends on 'ma' being general and the condition requiring the entire content to be male.
3
Analyze the dispute on 'min' in the divorce phrase
In 'Divorce yourself from three, whatever you wish' (Talki nafsaki min thalath ma shi'ti), the two Sahibayn interpret 'min' as explanatory (bayaniyyah), so 'from three' means 'three'. Imam Abu Hanifa interprets it as partitive (tab'idiyyah), so it means 'from three', i.e., less than three.
The disagreement is over the default meaning of 'min' when combined with 'ma'.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Sahibayn's view, the wife may divorce three times. Under Imam Abu Hanifa's view, she may only divorce less than three. This directly affects the validity of a triple divorce when the husband delegated the choice.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; both positions are valid within the school.
5
Synthesize the usul lesson
The text teaches that particles like 'ma' and 'min' have default meanings that can be overridden by context. The Hanafi method is to preserve the literal meaning unless strong evidence prompts a figurative interpretation.
This analysis is foundational for istinbat (derivation) in usul al-fiqh.

تشریح : منھا ما:۔ الفاظ عموم میں سے ایک ما بھی ہے۔ لیکن یہ ما غیر ذوی المعقول میں استعمال ہوتا ہے ۔ البتہ کبھی اس کو "من" کے معنی کے لیے مستعار لیا جاتا ہے تو اس وقت یہ عقلاء میں استعمال ہوتا ہے لیکن یہ مجاز کے قبیل سے ہے حقیقہ غیر عقلاء میں استعمال ہوتا ہے جیسے اگر کوئی کہے ان کان مانی بطنک غلاما فانت حرۃ اور اس لونڈی نے بچہ اور بچی اکٹھے جن دیئے تو وہ آزاد نہیں ہوگی۔ اس لیے کہ یہاں ما‘ عام ہے اور یہ تمام اس چیز کو شامل ہے جو اس کے پیٹ میں ہے اور اس کلام کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو کچھ تیرے پیٹ میں ہے وہ تمام اگر بچہ ہوا تو تو آزاد ہے اور مذکورہ صورت میں جمیع مافی البطن غلام اور بچہ نہیں بلکہ بعض مافی السیطن غلام ہے اور بعض مافی البطن جاریہ ہے اس لئے شرط حریت پوری نہیں ہوتی اس لئے آزاد نہیں ہوگی اگر ایک آدمی نے کہا " طلقی نفسک من ثلاث ما شئتِ " تو صاحبین کے نزدیک اس عورت کو تین طلاقیں دینے کا اختیار ہوگا اور امام صاحب کے نزدیک تین سے کم کم طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ وجہ وہی ہے کہ صاحبین کے نزدیک یہاں ما عام ہے اور من بیانیہ ہے لہذا امن ٹکٹ کا بیان ہوگا اور وہ کل طلاق مشروعہ دے سکتی ہے۔ اور امام صاحب کے ہاں ما عام ہے لیکن من تبعیضیہ ہے تو دونوں پر عمل کریں گے اور یہ اس صورت میں ممکن ہے کہ اس کو تین سے کم طلاقیں دینے کا اختیار ہو۔)
"And among them is 'ma' which is used for non-intelligent beings, and it may be borrowed for 'man'. For if he said: 'If what is in your womb is a male, then you are free,' and she gives birth to a male and a female, she is not freed because the intended meaning is totality. And if he said: 'Divorce yourself from three, whatever you wish,' she divorces what is less than that, and according to the two it is three, and it has passed." The rest is Urdu commentary expanding on these rulings.
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of the general particle 'ma'. It belongs to the usul topic of general words (alfaz al-'umum). The text clarifies that 'ma' is originally used for non-intelligent beings (ghayr dhawial-'uqul) but can be borrowed metaphorically for intelligent beings. Two illustrative fiqh rulings are given: 1) If a man says to his slave-girl 'If what is in your womb is a male, you are free,' and she gives birth to both a male and a female, she is not freed because 'ma' is general, encompassing everything in her womb, and the condition requires the totality to be male. Since it is not, the condition fails. 2) If a man says to his wife 'Divorce yourself from three, whatever you wish' (min thalathin ma shi'ti), a dispute arises: Imam Abu Hanifa ('indahu) holds she may divorce only less than three, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, 'indahuma) hold she may divorce three. The reasoning is that 'ma' is general and 'min' is explanatory (bayaniyyah) according to the Sahibayn, so she can divorce the full three. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'min' is partitive (tab'idiyyah), so both general and partitive meanings must be respected, resulting in the option to divorce less than three.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A general particle used primarily for non-intelligent beings, encompassing all individuals of a category.
من (بیانیہ) (min bayaniyyah): A preposition used to specify or explain, here indicating the nature of the object (e.g., 'from three' meaning 'three').
من (تبعیضیہ) (min tab'idiyyah): A preposition indicating part of a whole, implying limitation to a portion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why can the wife only divorce less than three in 'Talki nafsaki min thalath ma shi'ti'?
A) Because 'ma' is ambiguous.
B) Because 'min' is partitive, so the ruling must preserve both the generality of 'ma' and the partitivity of 'min'.
C) Because the wife's intention is unknown.
D) Because divorce is not allowed in threes.

Key Takeaway
The generality of 'ma' is a powerful tool in usul, but its interaction with other particles like 'min' requires careful attention to default meanings and contextual evidence; the mufta bihi in the divorce dispute is the Sahibayn's view.

The Definitive Generality of 'Kull' and 'Jami''
Explanation of the Hanafi rule on the definitiveness of 'kull' and 'jami''.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that 'kull' and 'jami'' are definitive in generality and apply it to distinguish between individual and total generality.

Core Idea
Kull and jami' are muhkaman – they cover each individual separately when attached to a nakirah.

In Hanafi usul, not all words of generality are equal. While tools like 'man' and 'ma' are zanni, 'kull' and 'jami'' are considered definitive (muhkam), meaning their generality cannot be restricted without specific evidence. This rule has direct implications for interpreting legal statements and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two words and their classification
Recognize that 'kull' and 'jami'' are singled out as muhkaman (definitive) in generality, unlike other tools of generality.
Muhkam is the highest level of clarity in the Hanafi text hierarchy.
2
Distinguish the effect on indefinite vs definite nouns
Understand that when attached to a nakirah (indefinite noun), they indicate generality of individuals (each individual separately). When attached to a ma'rifah (definite noun), they indicate totality (the whole group).
This distinction is key for correct application.
3
Apply the rule to the example of simultaneous entry
Analyze the scenario where ten people enter a fortress at the same time after a promise to the 'first' entrant. Under 'kull', each entrant is considered 'first' relative to those behind, so all are entitled.
This illustrates 'ala sabili al-infiraad' (individual generality).
4
Grasp the unique distinction when 'kull' is added to 'man dakhala awwala'
Understand the author's point: 'man dakhala awwala' alone indicates substitution generality (only one person). Adding 'kull' necessitates a second generality to avoid redundancy, resulting in multiple 'first' persons.
This nuance prevents the word 'kull' from being meaningless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهَا كُلُّ وَجَمِيعُ وَهُمَا مُحْكَمَانِ فِي عُمُومِ مَا دَخَلَا عَلَيْهِ بِخِلَافِ سَائِرِ أدَوَاتِ الْعُمُومِ فَإِن دَخَلَ الْحَلُّ عَلَى النَّكِرَةِ فَلِعُمُومِ الْأَفْرَادِ وَإِنْ دَخَلَ عَلَى الْمَعْرِفَةِ فَلِلْمَجْمُوعِ قَالُوا عُمُومُهُ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الانْفِرَادِ أَى يُرَادُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ مَعَ قَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنْ غَيْرِهِ, وَهَذَا إِذَا دَخَلَ عَلَى النَّكِرَةِ فَإِنْ قَالَ كُلُّ مَنْ دَخَلَ هَذَا الْحِصْنَ أَوَّلا فَلَهُ كَذَا فَدَخَلَ عَشَرَةٌ مَعًا يَسْتَحِقُ كُلُّ وَاحِدٍ إِذْ فِي كُلِّ فَرْدٍ قُطِعَ النَّظَرُ عَنْ غَيْرِهِ فَكُلُّ وَاحِدٍ أَوَّلُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْمُتَخَلْفِ بِخِلَافِ مَنْ دَخَلَ وَهَاهُنَا فَرْق آخَرُ هُوَ أَنَّ مَنْ دَخَلَ أَوْلَا عَامٌ عَلَى سَبِيلِ الْبَدَلِ فَإِذَا أَضَافَ الْكُلَّ إِلَيْهِ اقْتَضَى عُمُومًا آخَرَ لِئَلَّا يَلْغُوَ فَيَقْتَضِي الْعُمُومَ فِي الْأَوَّلِ فَيَتَعَدَّدُ الْأَوَّلُ ، وَهَذَا الْفَرْقُ قَدْ تَفَرَّدْتُ بِهِ أَيْضًا وَتَحْقِيقُهُ أَنَّ الْأَوَّلَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ الْفِرْدِ السَّابِقِ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى كُلِّ
"And among them are 'kull' and 'jami'', and they are definitive (muhkaman) in the generality of what they enter upon, contrary to other tools of generality. For if they enter upon a masculine [indefinite] noun, then it is for the generality of individuals; and if they enter upon a definite noun, then it is for the totality. They said: their generality is on the basis of singularity, meaning each individual is intended while disregarding others, and this is when they enter upon an indefinite noun. So if he said: 'Everyone who enters this fortress first will have such-and-such,' and ten entered together, each one is entitled because in each individual, disregard of others is considered, so each one is 'first' relative to those behind, contrary to 'man dakhala'. And here is another distinction: that 'man dakhala awwala' is general on the basis of substitution (badal); if 'kull' is added to it, it necessitates another generality so that it is not rendered useless; thus it necessitates generality in the first (awwala), so the 'first' becomes multiple. And this distinction I have uniquely mentioned, and its verification is that 'awwal' expresses the preceding individual relative to each [person]."
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage states a usul rule: the words 'kull' (كل) and 'jami'' (جميع) are definitive (muhkaman) in indicating the generality of the noun they are attached to, unlike other general tools. When they attach to an indefinite noun (nakirah), they indicate generality of individuals (each individual is intended). When they attach to a definite noun (ma'rifah), they indicate totality (the whole group). The commentators further explain that this individual generality is 'ala sabili al-infiraad', meaning each individual is considered independently. An example: if one says 'kullu man dakhala hadha al-hisna awwala falahu kadha' (everyone who enters this fortress first gets such-and-such), and ten enter at once, each one is entitled because each person can be considered 'first' relative to those who entered later. This differs from the phrase 'man dakhala awwala' (whoever enters first), which is general on a substitution basis (badal), implying only one person. When 'kull' is added to 'man dakhala awwala', a second generality is required to avoid redundancy, resulting in multiple 'first' persons. The author claims this distinction is unique to him in this work.

Key Terms
كل (كُلّ) (kull): A word indicating the totality of individuals or the whole, definitive in generality.
جميع (جَمِيع) (jami'): A word similar to 'kull' indicating the whole, definitive in generality.
محكم (muhkam): A text that is clear and definitive in meaning, not subject to abrogation or interpretation.
على سبيل الانفراد (ala sabili al-infiraad): Indicating each individual separately, disregarding others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'kullu man dakhala hadha al-hisna awwala falahu kadha' and ten people enter at the same time, how many receive the reward?
A) Only one, the first among them.
B) None, because entry was simultaneous.
C) All ten, because each can be considered first relative to those behind.
D) Only the one designated by the speaker.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'kull' and 'jami'' are definitive in generality, meaning their coverage of each individual is certain and can only be overridden by clear specification.

The Generality of 'Ma' in Conditional Statements
Analysis of 'ma' as a general term with a conditional example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify 'ma' as a general term (amm) and apply the Hanafi rule that it encompasses all its referents collectively to determine the fulfillment of a conditional statement.

Core Idea
'Ma' is general: a condition must be met for every part of its referent.

In Hanafi usul, 'ma' is a general term used for non-rational beings. Its generality means that when it appears in a condition, the condition applies to every individual item covered by 'ma'. The first example in our passage demonstrates this: a man says to his slave-girl, 'If what (ma) is in your womb is a male slave, you are free.' She gives birth to twins—one male, one female. Because 'ma' includes the entire content, the condition requires that every child be male. Since one is female, the condition fails and she is not freed. This lesson will walk you through the rule and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify 'ma' as a general term
Recognize that 'ma' in the passage is one of the words of umum (general terms). Its default usage is for non-rational beings (ghayr dhawi al-‘uqul).
Other general terms include 'man' (who) for rational beings.
2
Understand the principle of generality
A general term (amm) in Hanafi usul applies to all its individual instances without exception, unless a specific exclusion (takhsis) is proven.
This is the rule 'al-amm yufidu al-umum.'
3
Apply the principle to the slave-girl example
The statement 'If what is in your womb is a male slave' uses 'ma' to refer to everything in the womb. The condition requires that the entire content be a male slave. Since the birth includes a female, the condition is not fulfilled.
The ruling: she is not freed, because the condition is not met for all parts.
4
Derive the ruling from the textual analysis
The generality of 'ma' leads to the conclusion that the woman does not become free. This shows that 'ma' retains its full generality unless restricted by context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح : منھا ما:۔ الفاظ عموم میں سے ایک ما بھی ہے۔ لیکن یہ ما غیر ذوی المعقول میں استعمال ہوتا ہے ۔ البتہ کبھی اس کو "من" کے معنی کے لیے مستعار لیا جاتا ہے تو اس وقت یہ عقلاء میں استعمال ہوتا ہے لیکن یہ مجاز کے قبیل سے ہے حقیقہ غیر عقلاء میں استعمال ہوتا ہے جیسے اگر کوئی کہے ان کان مانی بطنک غلاما فانت حرۃ اور اس لونڈی نے بچہ اور بچی اکٹھے جن دیئے تو وہ آزاد نہیں ہوگی۔ اس لیے کہ یہاں ما‘ عام ہے اور یہ تمام اس چیز کو شامل ہے جو اس کے پیٹ میں ہے اور اس کلام کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو کچھ تیرے پیٹ میں ہے وہ تمام اگر بچہ ہوا تو تو آزاد ہے اور مذکورہ صورت میں جمیع مافی البطن غلام اور بچہ نہیں بلکہ بعض مافی السیطن غلام ہے اور بعض مافی البطن جاریہ ہے اس لئے شرط حریت پوری نہیں ہوتی اس لئے آزاد نہیں ہوگی اگر ایک آدمی نے کہا " طلقی نفسک من ثلاث ما شئتِ " تو صاحبین کے نزدیک اس عورت کو تین طلاقیں دینے کا اختیار ہوگا اور امام صاحب کے نزدیک تین سے کم کم طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ وجہ وہی ہے کہ صاحبین کے نزدیک یہاں ما عام ہے اور من بیانیہ ہے لہذا امن ٹکٹ کا بیان ہوگا اور وہ کل طلاق مشروعہ دے سکتی ہے۔ اور امام صاحب کے ہاں ما عام ہے لیکن من تبعیضیہ ہے تو دونوں پر عمل کریں گے اور یہ اس صورت میں ممکن ہے کہ اس کو تین سے کم طلاقیں دینے کا اختیار ہو۔
Explanation: Among them is 'ma'. The word 'ma' is also one of the general terms. But this 'ma' is used for non-rational beings. However, sometimes it is borrowed in the meaning of 'man', so at that time it is used for rational beings, but this is from the category of metaphor; in reality it is used for non-rational beings. For example, if someone says, 'If what is in your womb is a male slave, you are free,' and that slave-girl gives birth to a male and a female together, then she will not be free. This is because here 'ma' is general and it includes everything that is in her womb. The meaning of the statement is that everything that is in your womb, if it is a male child, then you are free. In the mentioned case, the entirety of what is in the womb is not a male child, but rather part of what is in the womb is a male and part is a female. Therefore the condition for freedom is not fulfilled, so she will not be free. If a man said, 'Divorce yourself from me by three as you wish,' then according to the Sahibayn, that woman will have the option to give three divorces, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa, she will have the option to give less than three divorces. The reason is the same: according to the Sahibayn, here 'ma' is general and 'min' is declaratory (bayaniyyah), so the statement will be explanatory and she can give all the legislated divorces. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'ma' is general but 'min' is partitive (tab'idiyyah), so both will be acted upon, and this is possible in the case where she has the option to give less than three divorces.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis explaining the word 'ma' as one of the general terms (alfaz al-‘umum) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author clarifies that 'ma' is primarily used for non-rational beings (ghayr dhawi al-‘uqul), i.e., things, animals, objects. However, it can be borrowed metaphorically to mean 'man' (who) for rational beings. The first example illustrates a conditional statement: 'If what is in your womb is a male slave, you are free.' The slave-girl gives birth to twins, one male and one female. Since 'ma' is general and covers the entire content of the womb, the condition requires that everything in the womb be a male. Since only part is male, the condition is not fulfilled, and she is not freed. This shows that 'ma' is general and applies to each individual item collectively. The second example involves a man giving his wife the option to divorce herself: 'Divorce yourself from me by three, as you wish.' There is a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The Sahibayn interpret 'min' as bayaniyyah (declaratory) and 'ma' as general, so the wife may give three divorces (the complete number mentioned). Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'min' as tab'idiyyah (partitive) and 'ma' as general, requiring acting on both: the wife may give less than three divorces, because the partitive 'min' indicates a portion of the three. This ikhtilaf turns on the interpretation of 'min' and the scope of 'ma'.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A general term (min alfaz al-‘umum) used for non-rational beings; sometimes borrowed for rational beings metaphorically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the slave-girl example, why is she not freed when she gives birth to both a male and a female?
A) Because 'ma' is not general in this context.
B) Because the condition requires that every part of 'ma' be a male slave, and part is female.
C) Because the female child invalidates the entire condition.
D) Because the man's intention was only for a single male child.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'ma' as a general term demands that a condition be fulfilled for every individual referred to by 'ma'.

The Hanafi Ikhtilaf on 'Min': Bayaniyyah vs Tab'idiyyah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the interpretation of 'min' in a divorce delegation phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the interpretation of 'min' in the phrase 'min thalath ma shi't' and evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) in divorce options.

Core Idea
Interpretation of 'min' as partitive vs. declaratory changes the wife's divorce option.

A key intra-Hanafi dispute involves the particle 'min' in the phrase 'min thalath ma shi't' (divorce yourself by three as you wish). Imam Abu Hanifa views 'min' as partitive (tab'idiyyah), so the wife may give less than three divorces. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) view 'min' as declaratory (bayaniyyah), so she may give three divorces. This ikhtilaf turns on how the generality of 'ma' interacts with 'min'. In this lesson, you will dissect the arguments and understand the practical ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions clearly
Sahibayn: wife may give three divorces. Imam Abu Hanifa: wife may give less than three divorces.
Note that both agree 'ma' is general; the disagreement is only on 'min'.
2
Identify the disputed particle and its interpretations
The particle 'min' can be bayaniyyah (explanatory, defining the number as exactly three) or tab'idiyyah (partitive, indicating a portion of the number).
The context of divorce makes this difference legally significant.
3
Analyze the reasoning of the Sahibayn
They treat 'min' as bayaniyyah, so 'ma' remains general and includes all three divorces. The phrase 'min thalath' merely explains that the option is for three divorces. Therefore, the wife can execute all three.
Their dalil: the generality of 'ma' is unrestricted by 'min'.
4
Analyze the reasoning of Imam Abu Hanifa
He treats 'min' as tab'idiyyah, so the generality of 'ma' is restricted to a part of the number. To give effect to both 'ma' (general) and 'min' (partitive), the wife may only give less than three divorces, because 'min' indicates a portion of the mentioned number.
His dalil: both words must be operative; 'min' limits the scope.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The wife's authority to execute three divorces versus less than three. This affects the finality of divorce: a less-than-three divorce is revocable, while three is irrevocable.
The text does not give a preference, so muftis might choose based on other principles.

تشریح : منھا ما:۔ الفاظ عموم میں سے ایک ما بھی ہے۔ لیکن یہ ما غیر ذوی المعقول میں استعمال ہوتا ہے ۔ البتہ کبھی اس کو "من" کے معنی کے لیے مستعار لیا جاتا ہے تو اس وقت یہ عقلاء میں استعمال ہوتا ہے لیکن یہ مجاز کے قبیل سے ہے حقیقہ غیر عقلاء میں استعمال ہوتا ہے جیسے اگر کوئی کہے ان کان مانی بطنک غلاما فانت حرۃ اور اس لونڈی نے بچہ اور بچی اکٹھے جن دیئے تو وہ آزاد نہیں ہوگی۔ اس لیے کہ یہاں ما‘ عام ہے اور یہ تمام اس چیز کو شامل ہے جو اس کے پیٹ میں ہے اور اس کلام کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جو کچھ تیرے پیٹ میں ہے وہ تمام اگر بچہ ہوا تو تو آزاد ہے اور مذکورہ صورت میں جمیع مافی البطن غلام اور بچہ نہیں بلکہ بعض مافی السیطن غلام ہے اور بعض مافی البطن جاریہ ہے اس لئے شرط حریت پوری نہیں ہوتی اس لئے آزاد نہیں ہوگی اگر ایک آدمی نے کہا " طلقی نفسک من ثلاث ما شئتِ " تو صاحبین کے نزدیک اس عورت کو تین طلاقیں دینے کا اختیار ہوگا اور امام صاحب کے نزدیک تین سے کم کم طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ وجہ وہی ہے کہ صاحبین کے نزدیک یہاں ما عام ہے اور من بیانیہ ہے لہذا امن ٹکٹ کا بیان ہوگا اور وہ کل طلاق مشروعہ دے سکتی ہے۔ اور امام صاحب کے ہاں ما عام ہے لیکن من تبعیضیہ ہے تو دونوں پر عمل کریں گے اور یہ اس صورت میں ممکن ہے کہ اس کو تین سے کم طلاقیں دینے کا اختیار ہو۔
Explanation: Among them is 'ma'. The word 'ma' is also one of the general terms. But this 'ma' is used for non-rational beings. However, sometimes it is borrowed in the meaning of 'man', so at that time it is used for rational beings, but this is from the category of metaphor; in reality it is used for non-rational beings. For example, if someone says, 'If what is in your womb is a male slave, you are free,' and that slave-girl gives birth to a male and a female together, then she will not be free. This is because here 'ma' is general and it includes everything that is in her womb. The meaning of the statement is that everything that is in your womb, if it is a male child, then you are free. In the mentioned case, the entirety of what is in the womb is not a male child, but rather part of what is in the womb is a male and part is a female. Therefore the condition for freedom is not fulfilled, so she will not be free. If a man said, 'Divorce yourself from me by three as you wish,' then according to the Sahibayn, that woman will have the option to give three divorces, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa, she will have the option to give less than three divorces. The reason is the same: according to the Sahibayn, here 'ma' is general and 'min' is declaratory (bayaniyyah), so the statement will be explanatory and she can give all the legislated divorces. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, 'ma' is general but 'min' is partitive (tab'idiyyah), so both will be acted upon, and this is possible in the case where she has the option to give less than three divorces.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis explaining the word 'ma' as one of the general terms (alfaz al-‘umum) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author clarifies that 'ma' is primarily used for non-rational beings (ghayr dhawi al-‘uqul), i.e., things, animals, objects. However, it can be borrowed metaphorically to mean 'man' (who) for rational beings. The first example illustrates a conditional statement: 'If what is in your womb is a male slave, you are free.' The slave-girl gives birth to twins, one male and one female. Since 'ma' is general and covers the entire content of the womb, the condition requires that everything in the womb be a male. Since only part is male, the condition is not fulfilled, and she is not freed. This shows that 'ma' is general and applies to each individual item collectively. The second example involves a man giving his wife the option to divorce herself: 'Divorce yourself from me by three, as you wish.' There is a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The Sahibayn interpret 'min' as bayaniyyah (declaratory) and 'ma' as general, so the wife may give three divorces (the complete number mentioned). Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'min' as tab'idiyyah (partitive) and 'ma' as general, requiring acting on both: the wife may give less than three divorces, because the partitive 'min' indicates a portion of the three. This ikhtilaf turns on the interpretation of 'min' and the scope of 'ma'.

Key Terms
ما (ma): A general term (min alfaz al-‘umum) used for non-rational beings; sometimes borrowed for rational beings metaphorically.
من (min): A particle interpreted as bayaniyyah (declaratory) or tab'idiyyah (partitive) depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, how many divorces can a wife give if told 'Divorce yourself from me by three as you wish'?
A) Only one divorce
B) Up to three divorces
C) Exactly three divorces
D) None, because the phrase is invalid

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'min' as partitive or declaratory alters the scope of delegated authority in Hanafi law.
